<p><b>Dinosaurs</b> are a diverse group of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cold-blooded_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-cold-blooded-1">[note 1]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <b>Dinosauria</b>. They first appeared during the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> period, between 243 and 233.23 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langer18_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-langer18-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alcobar2010_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alcobar2010-3">[2]</a></sup> although the exact origin and timing of the evolution of dinosaurs is the subject of active research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesbitt2012_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesbitt2012-4">[3]</a></sup> They became the dominant terrestrial vertebrates after the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a> 201 million years ago; their dominance continued through the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods. Reverse genetic engineering<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record both demonstrate that <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> are modern <a href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaur" title="Feathered dinosaur">feathered dinosaurs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-6">[5]</a></sup> having <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_birds" title="Evolution of birds">evolved</a> from earlier <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a> during the late Jurassic Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140731_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140731-7">[6]</a></sup> As such, birds were the only dinosaur lineage to survive the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> 66 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz2007_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holtz2007-8">[7]</a></sup> Dinosaurs can therefore be divided into <i>avian dinosaurs</i>, or birds; and <i>non-avian dinosaurs</i>, which are all dinosaurs other than birds. This article deals primarily with non-avian dinosaurs.
</p><p>Dinosaurs are a varied group of animals from <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> standpoints. Birds, at over 10,000 living species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> are the most diverse group of vertebrates besides <a href="/wiki/Perciformes" title="Perciformes">perciform</a> fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alfaroetal2009_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alfaroetal2009-10">[9]</a></sup> Using fossil evidence, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontologists</a> have identified over 500 distinct <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang&amp;Dodson_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang&amp;Dodson-11">[10]</a></sup> and more than 1,000 different species of non-avian dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmosBBC_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmosBBC-12">[11]</a></sup> Dinosaurs are represented on every continent by both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species (birds) and fossil remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-13">[12]</a></sup> Through the first half of the 20th century, before birds were recognized to be dinosaurs, most of the scientific community believed dinosaurs to have been sluggish and <a href="/wiki/Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm">cold-blooded</a>. Most <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_renaissance" title="Dinosaur renaissance">research conducted since the 1970s</a>, however, has indicated that all dinosaurs were active animals with elevated <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolisms</a> and numerous adaptations for social interaction. Some were herbivorous, others carnivorous. Evidence suggests that egg-laying and nest-building are additional traits shared by all dinosaurs, avian and non-avian alike.
</p><p>While dinosaurs were ancestrally <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">bipedal</a>, many extinct groups included <a href="/wiki/Quadrupedalism" title="Quadrupedalism">quadrupedal</a> species, and some were able to shift between these stances. Elaborate display structures such as horns or crests are common to all dinosaur groups, and some extinct groups developed skeletal modifications such as <a href="/wiki/Armour_(anatomy)" title="Armour (anatomy)">bony armor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spine_(zoology)" title="Spine (zoology)">spines</a>. While the dinosaurs' modern-day surviving avian lineage (birds) are generally small due to the constraints of flight, many prehistoric dinosaurs (non-avian and avian) were large-bodied—the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropod" title="Sauropod">sauropod</a> dinosaurs are estimated to have reached lengths of 39.7 meters (130 feet)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> and heights of 18 meters (59 feet)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-15">[14]</a></sup> and were the largest land animals of all time. Still, the idea that non-avian dinosaurs were uniformly gigantic is a misconception based in part on preservation bias, as large, sturdy bones are more likely to last until they are fossilized. Many dinosaurs were quite small: <i><a href="/wiki/Xixianykus" title="Xixianykus">Xixianykus</a></i>, for example, was only about 50 cm (20 in) long.
</p><p>Since the first dinosaur <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> were recognized in the early 19th century, mounted fossil dinosaur skeletons have been major attractions at museums around the world, and dinosaurs have become an enduring part of world culture. The large sizes of some dinosaur groups, as well as their seemingly monstrous and fantastic nature, have ensured dinosaurs' regular appearance in best-selling books and films, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">Jurassic Park</a></i>. Persistent public enthusiasm for the animals has resulted in significant funding for dinosaur science, and new discoveries are regularly covered by the media.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a> 'Dinosauria' was formally named in 1841 by <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Sir Richard Owen</a>, who used it to refer to the "distinct tribe or sub-order of Saurian Reptiles" that were then being recognized in England and around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen1841_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen1841-16">[15]</a></sup> The term is derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B4%CE%B5%CE%B9%CE%BD%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:δεινός">δεινός</a><i> (deinos)</i>, meaning 'terrible, potent or fearfully great', and <i> </i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%83%CE%B1%E1%BF%A6%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:σαῦρος">σαῦρος</a><i> (sauros)</i>, meaning 'lizard or reptile'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen1841_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Owen1841-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ-17">[16]</a></sup> Though the taxonomic name has often been interpreted as a reference to dinosaurs' teeth, claws, and other fearsome characteristics, Owen intended it merely to evoke their size and majesty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FBS97_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FBS97-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other prehistoric animals, including <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur">mosasaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur">ichthyosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plesiosauria" title="Plesiosauria">plesiosaurs</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon">Dimetrodon</a></i>, while often popularly conceived of as dinosaurs, are not <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomically</a> classified as dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-19">[18]</a></sup> Pterosaurs are distantly related to dinosaurs, being members of the clade <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithodira" title="Ornithodira">Ornithodira</a>. The other groups mentioned are, like dinosaurs and pterosaurs, members of <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a> (the reptile and bird <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>), with the exception of <i>Dimetrodon</i> (which is a <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">synapsid</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3460" data-file-width="4560" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg/250px-LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg/375px-LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg/500px-LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LA-Triceratops_mount-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> skeleton, <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum_of_Los_Angeles_County" title="Natural History Museum of Los Angeles County">Natural History Museum of Los Angeles County</a></div></div></div>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">phylogenetic nomenclature</a>, dinosaurs are usually defined as the group consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> (MRCA) of <i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_birds" title="Modern birds">Neornithes</a>, and all its descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-20">[19]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that Dinosauria be defined with respect to the MRCA of <i><a href="/wiki/Megalosaurus" title="Megalosaurus">Megalosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i>, because these were two of the three genera cited by Richard Owen when he recognized the Dinosauria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olshevsky2000_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-olshevsky2000-21">[20]</a></sup> Both definitions result in the same set of animals being defined as dinosaurs: "Dinosauria = <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischia</a> + <a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">Saurischia</a>", encompassing <a href="/wiki/Ankylosauria" title="Ankylosauria">ankylosaurians</a> (armored herbivorous quadrupeds), <a href="/wiki/Stegosauria" title="Stegosauria">stegosaurians</a> (plated herbivorous quadrupeds), <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">ceratopsians</a> (herbivorous quadrupeds with horns and frills), <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">ornithopods</a> (bipedal or quadrupedal herbivores including "duck-bills"), <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">bipedal</a> <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Sauropodomorpha" title="Sauropodomorpha">sauropodomorphs</a> (mostly large <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadruped" title="Quadruped">quadrupeds</a> with long necks and tails).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letal05_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letal05-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Birds are now recognized as being the sole surviving lineage of theropod dinosaurs. In traditional taxonomy, birds were considered a separate <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> that had evolved from dinosaurs, a distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superorder" title="Superorder">superorder</a>. However, a majority of contemporary paleontologists concerned with dinosaurs reject the traditional style of classification in favor of phylogenetic taxonomy; this approach requires that, for a group to be natural, all descendants of members of the group must be included in the group as well. Birds are thus considered to be dinosaurs and dinosaurs are, therefore, not extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Birds are classified as belonging to the subgroup <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">Maniraptora</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">coelurosaurs</a>, which are theropods, which are <a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">saurischians</a>, which are dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basalavialae_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-basalavialae-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research by Matthew Baron, David B. Norman, and Paul M. Barrett in 2017 suggested a radical revision of dinosaurian systematics. Phylogenetic analysis by Baron <i>et al.</i> recovered the Ornithischia as being closer to the Theropoda than the Sauropodomorpha, as opposed to the traditional union of theropods with sauropodomorphs. They resurrected the clade <a href="/wiki/Ornithoscelida" title="Ornithoscelida">Ornithoscelida</a> to refer to the group containing Ornithischia and Theropoda. Dinosauria itself was re-defined as the last common ancestor of <i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops horridus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">Passer domesticus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Diplodocus" title="Diplodocus">Diplodocus carnegii</a></i>, and all of its descendants, to ensure that sauropods and kin remain included as dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170322_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170322-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170322_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170322-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_description">General description</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neognathae.jpg"><img alt="montage of four birds" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1000" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Neognathae.jpg/220px-Neognathae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Neognathae.jpg/330px-Neognathae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Neognathae.jpg/440px-Neognathae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neognathae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> taxonomy, birds are included in the group Dinosauria.</div></div></div>
<p>Using one of the above definitions, dinosaurs can be generally described as <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a> with <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_locomotion#Posture" title="Terrestrial locomotion">hind limbs held erect beneath the body</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG97_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-DFG97-27">[26]</a></sup> Many prehistoric animal groups are popularly conceived of as dinosaurs, such as <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur">ichthyosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur">mosasaurs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plesiosaur" title="Plesiosaur">plesiosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pelycosaur" title="Pelycosaur">pelycosaurs</a> (especially <i><a href="/wiki/Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon">Dimetrodon</a></i>), but are not classified scientifically as dinosaurs, and none had the erect hind limb posture characteristic of true dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL90_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-DL90-28">[27]</a></sup> Dinosaurs were the dominant terrestrial vertebrates of the Mesozoic, especially the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods. Other groups of animals were restricted in size and niches; mammals, for example, rarely exceeded the size of a domestic cat, and were generally rodent-sized carnivores of small prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM97_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-MM97-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dinosaurs have always been an extremely varied group of animals; according to a 2006 study, over 500 non-avian dinosaur genera have been identified with certainty so far, and the total number of genera preserved in the fossil record has been estimated at around 1850, nearly 75% of which remain to be discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang&amp;Dodson_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang&amp;Dodson-11">[10]</a></sup> An earlier study predicted that about 3,400 dinosaur genera existed, including many that would not have been preserved in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell1995_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell1995-30">[29]</a></sup> By September 17, 2008, 1,047 different species of dinosaurs had been named.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmosBBC_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-AmosBBC-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the estimated number of dinosaur species that existed in the Mesozoic era was estimated to be 1,543–2,468.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Some are herbivorous, others carnivorous, including seed-eaters, fish-eaters, insectivores, and omnivores. While dinosaurs were ancestrally bipedal (as are all modern birds), some prehistoric species were quadrupeds, and others, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Anchisaurus" title="Anchisaurus">Anchisaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i>, could walk just as easily on two or four legs. Cranial modifications like horns and crests are common dinosaurian traits, and some extinct species had <a href="/wiki/Armour_(anatomy)" title="Armour (anatomy)">bony armor</a>. Although known for large size, many Mesozoic dinosaurs were human-sized or smaller, and modern birds are generally small in size. Dinosaurs today inhabit every continent, and fossils show that they had achieved global distribution by at least the early Jurassic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-13">[12]</a></sup> Modern birds inhabit most available habitats, from terrestrial to marine, and there is evidence that some non-avian dinosaurs (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i>) could fly or at least glide, and others, such as <a href="/wiki/Spinosauridae" title="Spinosauridae">spinosaurids</a>, had <a href="/wiki/List_of_semiaquatic_tetrapods" title="List of semiaquatic tetrapods">semiaquatic</a> habits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMetal10_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-RMetal10-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinguishing_anatomical_features">Distinguishing anatomical features</span></h3>
<p>While recent discoveries have made it more difficult to present a universally agreed-upon list of dinosaurs' distinguishing features, nearly all dinosaurs discovered so far share certain modifications to the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurian</a> skeleton, or are clear descendants of older dinosaurs showing these modifications. Although some later groups of dinosaurs featured further modified versions of these traits, they are considered typical for Dinosauria; the earliest dinosaurs had them and passed them on to their descendants. Such modifications, originating in the <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a> of a certain taxonomic group, are called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapomorphy" title="Synapomorphy">synapomorphies</a> of such a group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B012_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-B012-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>A detailed assessment of archosaur interrelations by <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Nesbitt" title="Sterling Nesbitt">Sterling Nesbitt</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nesbitt2011_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-nesbitt2011-35">[34]</a></sup> confirmed or found the following twelve unambiguous synapomorphies, some previously known:
</p>
<ul><li>in the skull, a supratemporal fossa (excavation) is present in front of the supratemporal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temporal_fenestra#Temporal_fenestrae" title="Temporal fenestra">fenestra</a>, the main opening in the rear skull roof</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epipophyses" title="Epipophyses">epipophyses</a>, obliquely backward pointing processes on the rear top corners, present in the anterior (front) neck vertebrae behind the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(anatomy)" title="Atlas (anatomy)">atlas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axis_(anatomy)" title="Axis (anatomy)">axis</a>, the first two neck vertebrae</li>
<li>apex of deltopectoral crest (a projection on which the <a href="/wiki/Clavipectoral_triangle" title="Clavipectoral triangle">deltopectoral</a> muscles attach) located at or more than 30% down the length of the <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> (upper arm bone)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radius_(bone)" title="Radius (bone)">radius</a>, a lower arm bone, shorter than 80% of humerus length</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fourth_trochanter" title="Fourth trochanter">fourth trochanter</a> (projection where the <a href="/wiki/Caudofemoralis" title="Caudofemoralis">caudofemoralis</a> muscle attaches on the inner rear shaft) on the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> (thighbone) is a sharp flange</li>
<li>fourth trochanter asymmetrical, with distal, lower, margin forming a steeper angle to the shaft</li>
<li>on the <a href="/wiki/Talus_bone" title="Talus bone">astragalus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcaneus" title="Calcaneus">calcaneum</a>, upper ankle bones, the proximal articular facet, the top connecting surface, for the <a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">fibula</a> occupies less than 30% of the transverse width of the element</li>
<li>exoccipitals (bones at the back of the skull) do not meet along the midline on the floor of the endocranial cavity, the inner space of the braincase</li>
<li>in the pelvis, the proximal articular surfaces of the <a href="/wiki/Ischium" title="Ischium">ischium</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubis</a> are separated by a large concave surface (on the upper side of the ischium a part of the open hip joint is located between the contacts with the pubic bone and the ilium)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cnemial_crest" title="Cnemial crest">cnemial crest</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a> (protruding part of the top surface of the shinbone) arcs anterolaterally (curves to the front and the outer side)</li>
<li>distinct proximodistally oriented (vertical) ridge present on the posterior face of the distal end of the tibia (the rear surface of the lower end of the shinbone)</li>
<li>concave articular surface for the fibula of the calcaneum (the top surface of the calcaneum, where it touches the fibula, has a hollow profile)</li></ul>
<p>Nesbitt found a number of further potential synapomorphies, and discounted a number of synapomorphies previously suggested. Some of these are also present in <a href="/wiki/Silesauridae" title="Silesauridae">silesaurids</a>, which Nesbitt recovered as a sister group to Dinosauria, including a large anterior trochanter, metatarsals II and IV of subequal length, reduced contact between ischium and pubis, the presence of a cnemial crest on the tibia and of an ascending process on the astragalus, and many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skull_diapsida_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="257" data-file-width="558" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Skull_diapsida_1.svg/220px-Skull_diapsida_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Skull_diapsida_1.svg/330px-Skull_diapsida_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Skull_diapsida_1.svg/440px-Skull_diapsida_1.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skull_diapsida_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of a typical diapsid skull<br/>j: <a href="/wiki/Jugal_bone" title="Jugal bone">jugal bone</a>, po: <a href="/wiki/Postorbital_bone" title="Postorbital bone">postorbital bone</a>, p: <a href="/wiki/Parietal_bone" title="Parietal bone">parietal bone</a>, sq: <a href="/wiki/Squamosal_bone" title="Squamosal bone">squamosal bone</a>, q: <a href="/wiki/Quadrate_bone" title="Quadrate bone">quadrate bone</a>, qj: <a href="/wiki/Quadratojugal_bone" title="Quadratojugal bone">quadratojugal bone</a></div></div></div>
<p>A variety of other skeletal features are shared by dinosaurs. However, because they are either common to other groups of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archosaurs" title="Archosaurs">archosaurs</a> or were not present in all early dinosaurs, these features are not considered to be synapomorphies. For example, as <a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">diapsids</a>, dinosaurs ancestrally had two pairs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temporal_fenestra" title="Temporal fenestra">temporal fenestrae</a> (openings in the skull behind the eyes), and as members of the diapsid group Archosauria, had additional openings in the <a href="/wiki/Antorbital_fenestra" title="Antorbital fenestra">snout</a> and lower jaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRHJ00_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-TRHJ00-36">[35]</a></sup> Additionally, several characteristics once thought to be synapomorphies are now known to have appeared before dinosaurs, or were absent in the earliest dinosaurs and independently evolved by different dinosaur groups. These include an elongated <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapula</a>, or shoulder blade; a <a href="/wiki/Sacrum" title="Sacrum">sacrum</a> composed of three or more fused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_vertebra" title="Spinal vertebra">vertebrae</a> (three are found in some other archosaurs, but only two are found in <i><a href="/wiki/Herrerasaurus" title="Herrerasaurus">Herrerasaurus</a></i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-20">[19]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Acetabulum#Perforate_Acetabulum" title="Acetabulum">perforate acetabulum</a>, or hip socket, with a hole at the center of its inside surface (closed in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturnalia_(dinosaur)" title="Saturnalia (dinosaur)">Saturnalia</a></i>, for example).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UC_Berkeley_Journal_of_Earth_Sciences_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-UC_Berkeley_Journal_of_Earth_Sciences-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LARB99_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-LARB99-38">[37]</a></sup> Another difficulty of determining distinctly dinosaurian features is that early dinosaurs and other archosaurs from the late Triassic are often poorly known and were similar in many ways; these animals have sometimes been misidentified in the literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIP07_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIP07-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="492" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg/220px-Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg/330px-Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg/440px-Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sprawling_and_erect_hip_joints_-_horizontal.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hip joints and hindlimb postures of: (left to right) typical reptiles (sprawling), dinosaurs and mammals (erect), and <a href="/wiki/Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia">rauisuchians</a> (erect)</div></div></div>
<p>Dinosaurs stand with their hind limbs erect in a manner similar to <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_mammals#Erect_limbs" title="Evolution of mammals">most modern mammals</a>, but distinct from most other reptiles, whose limbs sprawl out to either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland1909_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland1909-40">[39]</a></sup> This posture is due to the development of a laterally facing recess in the pelvis (usually an open socket) and a corresponding inwardly facing distinct head on the femur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB00_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-MJB00-41">[40]</a></sup> Their erect posture enabled early dinosaurs to breathe easily while moving, which likely permitted stamina and activity levels that <a href="/wiki/Carrier%27s_constraint" title="Carrier's constraint">surpassed those of "sprawling" reptiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RC05_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-RC05-42">[41]</a></sup> Erect limbs probably also helped support the evolution of large size by reducing bending stresses on limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKMB07_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-TKMB07-43">[42]</a></sup> Some non-dinosaurian archosaurs, including <a href="/wiki/Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia">rauisuchians</a>, also had erect limbs but achieved this by a "pillar erect" configuration of the hip joint, where instead of having a projection from the femur insert on a socket on the hip, the <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">upper pelvic bone</a> was rotated to form an overhanging shelf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TKMB07_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-TKMB07-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_dinosaurs" title="Evolution of dinosaurs">Evolution of dinosaurs</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_evolution">Origins and early evolution</span></h3>
<p>Dinosaurs diverged from their <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaur</a> ancestors during the middle to late <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> period, roughly 20 million years after the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a> wiped out an estimated 95% of all <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KPA_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-KPA-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TannerLucas_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-TannerLucas-45">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">Radiometric dating</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_formation" title="Rock formation">rock formation</a> that contained fossils from the early dinosaur <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Eoraptor" title="Eoraptor">Eoraptor</a></i> at 231.4 million years old establishes its presence in the fossil record at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OARM2010_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-OARM2010-46">[45]</a></sup> Paleontologists think that <i>Eoraptor</i> resembles the <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a> of all dinosaurs;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno1999_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno1999-47">[46]</a></sup> if this is true, its traits suggest that the first dinosaurs were small, bipedal <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFRM93_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFRM93-48">[47]</a></sup> The discovery of primitive, dinosaur-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithodiran" title="Ornithodiran">ornithodirans</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Marasuchus" title="Marasuchus">Marasuchus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lagerpeton" title="Lagerpeton">Lagerpeton</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentinian</a> <a href="/wiki/Middle_Triassic" title="Middle Triassic">Middle Triassic</a> strata supports this view; analysis of recovered fossils suggests that these animals were indeed small, bipedal predators. Dinosaurs may have appeared as early as 243 million years ago, as evidenced by remains of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Nyasasaurus" title="Nyasasaurus">Nyasasaurus</a></i> from that period, though known fossils of these animals are too fragmentary to tell if they are dinosaurs or very close dinosaurian relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyasasaurus_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyasasaurus-49">[48]</a></sup> Recently, it has been determined that <i>Staurikosaurus</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_Formation" title="Santa Maria Formation">Santa Maria Formation</a> dates to 233.23 Ma, making it older in geologic age than <i>Eoraptor</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langer18_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-langer18-2">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>When dinosaurs appeared, they were not the dominant terrestrial animals. The terrestrial habitats were occupied by various types of <a href="/wiki/Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha">archosauromorphs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therapsida" title="Therapsida">therapsids</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Cynodont" title="Cynodont">cynodonts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhynchosaur" title="Rhynchosaur">rhynchosaurs</a>. Their main competitors were the <a href="/wiki/Pseudosuchia" title="Pseudosuchia">pseudosuchia</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aetosaur" title="Aetosaur">aetosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ornithosuchidae" title="Ornithosuchidae">ornithosuchids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia">rauisuchians</a>, which were more successful than the dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Most of these other animals became extinct in the Triassic, in one of two events. First, at about 215 million years ago, a variety of basal <a href="/wiki/Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha">archosauromorphs</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Protorosauria" title="Protorosauria">protorosaurs</a>, became extinct. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic–Jurassic extinction event">Triassic–Jurassic extinction event</a> (about 200 million years ago), that saw the end of most of the other groups of early archosaurs, like aetosaurs, ornithosuchids, <a href="/wiki/Phytosaur" title="Phytosaur">phytosaurs</a>, and rauisuchians. Rhynchosaurs and <a href="/wiki/Dicynodont" title="Dicynodont">dicynodonts</a> survived (at least in some areas) at least as late as early-mid <a href="/wiki/Norian" title="Norian">Norian</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Rhaetian" title="Rhaetian">Rhaetian</a>, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSN08_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSN08-52">[51]</a></sup> and the exact date of their extinction is uncertain. These losses left behind a land fauna of <a href="/wiki/Crocodylomorpha" title="Crocodylomorpha">crocodylomorphs</a>, dinosaurs, <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pterosauria" title="Pterosauria">pterosaurians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB04dino_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-MJB04dino-20">[19]</a></sup> The first few lines of early dinosaurs <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">diversified</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Carnian" title="Carnian">Carnian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norian" title="Norian">Norian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faunal_stage" title="Faunal stage">stages</a> of the Triassic, possibly by occupying the niches of the groups that became extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letal05_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Letal05-22">[21]</a></sup> Also notably, there was a heightened rate of extinction during the <a href="/wiki/Carnian_Pluvial_Event" title="Carnian Pluvial Event">Carnian Pluvial Event</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_and_paleobiogeography">Evolution and paleobiogeography</span></h3>
<p>Dinosaur evolution after the Triassic follows changes in vegetation and the location of continents. In the late Triassic and early Jurassic, the continents were connected as the single landmass <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, and there was a worldwide dinosaur fauna mostly composed of <a href="/wiki/Coelophysoidea" title="Coelophysoidea">coelophysoid</a> <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a> and early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropodomorph" title="Sauropodomorph">sauropodomorph</a> <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-54">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">Gymnosperm</a> plants (particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a>), a potential food source, radiated in the late Triassic. Early sauropodomorphs did not have sophisticated mechanisms for processing food in the mouth, and so must have employed other means of breaking down food farther along the digestive tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-55">[54]</a></sup> The general homogeneity of dinosaurian faunas continued into the middle and late Jurassic, where most localities had predators consisting of <a href="/wiki/Ceratosauria" title="Ceratosauria">ceratosaurians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinosauroidea" title="Spinosauroidea">spinosauroids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">carnosaurians</a>, and herbivores consisting of <a href="/wiki/Stegosauria" title="Stegosauria">stegosaurian</a> ornithischians and large sauropods. Examples of this include the <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a> of North America and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tendaguru" title="Tendaguru">Tendaguru Beds</a> of Tanzania. Dinosaurs in China show some differences, with specialized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinraptoridae" title="Sinraptoridae">sinraptorid</a> theropods and unusual, long-necked sauropods like <i><a href="/wiki/Mamenchisaurus" title="Mamenchisaurus">Mamenchisaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-54">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ankylosauria" title="Ankylosauria">Ankylosaurians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithopoda" title="Ornithopoda">ornithopods</a> were also becoming more common, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosauropods" title="Prosauropods">prosauropods</a> had become extinct. Conifers and <a href="/wiki/Pteridophyte" title="Pteridophyte">pteridophytes</a> were the most common plants. Sauropods, like the earlier prosauropods, were not oral processors, but ornithischians were evolving various means of dealing with food in the mouth, including potential <a href="/wiki/Cheek" title="Cheek">cheek</a>-like organs to keep food in the mouth, and jaw motions to grind food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-55">[54]</a></sup> Another notable evolutionary event of the Jurassic was the appearance of true birds, descended from <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">maniraptoran</a> <a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">coelurosaurians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marasuchus.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="668" data-file-width="1323" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Marasuchus.JPG/220px-Marasuchus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Marasuchus.JPG/330px-Marasuchus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Marasuchus.JPG/440px-Marasuchus.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marasuchus.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marasuchus_lilloensis" title="Marasuchus lilloensis">Marasuchus lilloensis</a></i>, a dinosaur-like <a href="/wiki/Avemetatarsalia" title="Avemetatarsalia">ornithodiran</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the early Cretaceous and the ongoing breakup of Pangaea, dinosaurs were becoming strongly differentiated by landmass. The earliest part of this time saw the spread of ankylosaurians, <a href="/wiki/Iguanodontia" title="Iguanodontia">iguanodontians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brachiosauridae" title="Brachiosauridae">brachiosaurids</a> through Europe, North America, and northern Africa. These were later supplemented or replaced in Africa by large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinosaurid" title="Spinosaurid">spinosaurid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosauridae" title="Carcharodontosauridae">carcharodontosaurid</a> theropods, and <a href="/wiki/Rebbachisauridae" title="Rebbachisauridae">rebbachisaurid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanosauria" title="Titanosauria">titanosaurian</a> sauropods, also found in South America. In Asia, maniraptoran coelurosaurians like <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troodontidae" title="Troodontidae">troodontids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oviraptorosauria" title="Oviraptorosauria">oviraptorosaurians</a> became the common theropods, and <a href="/wiki/Ankylosauridae" title="Ankylosauridae">ankylosaurids</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">ceratopsians</a> like <i>Psittacosaurus</i> became important herbivores. Meanwhile, Australia was home to a fauna of basal ankylosaurians, <a href="/wiki/Hypsilophodont" title="Hypsilophodont">hypsilophodonts</a>, and iguanodontians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-54">[53]</a></sup> The stegosaurians appear to have gone extinct at some point in the late early Cretaceous or early late Cretaceous. A major change in the early Cretaceous, which would be amplified in the late Cretaceous, was the evolution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperm" title="Angiosperm">flowering plants</a>. At the same time, several groups of dinosaurian herbivores evolved more sophisticated ways to orally process food. Ceratopsians developed a method of slicing with teeth stacked on each other in batteries, and iguanodontians refined a method of grinding with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinosaur_dental_histology" title="Dinosaur dental histology">tooth batteries</a>, taken to its extreme in <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurid" title="Hadrosaurid">hadrosaurids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-55">[54]</a></sup> Some sauropods also evolved tooth batteries, best exemplified by the rebbachisaurid <i><a href="/wiki/Nigersaurus" title="Nigersaurus">Nigersaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal07_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-serenoetal07-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg"><img alt="Full skeleton of an early carnivorous dinosaur, displayed in a glass case in a museum" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1604" data-file-width="2549" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg/220px-Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg/330px-Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg/440px-Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herrerasaurusskeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The early forms <i><a href="/wiki/Herrerasaurus" title="Herrerasaurus">Herrerasaurus</a></i> (large), <i><a href="/wiki/Eoraptor" title="Eoraptor">Eoraptor</a></i> (small) and a <i><a href="/wiki/Plateosaurus" title="Plateosaurus">Plateosaurus</a></i> skull</div></div></div>
<p>There were three general dinosaur faunas in the late Cretaceous. In the northern continents of North America and Asia, the major theropods were <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">tyrannosaurids</a> and various types of smaller maniraptoran theropods, with a predominantly ornithischian herbivore assemblage of hadrosaurids, ceratopsians, ankylosaurids, and <a href="/wiki/Pachycephalosauria" title="Pachycephalosauria">pachycephalosaurians</a>. In the southern continents that had made up the now-splitting <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abelisauridae" title="Abelisauridae">abelisaurids</a> were the common theropods, and titanosaurian sauropods the common herbivores. Finally, in Europe, dromaeosaurids, <a href="/wiki/Rhabdodontidae" title="Rhabdodontidae">rhabdodontid</a> iguanodontians, <a href="/wiki/Nodosauridae" title="Nodosauridae">nodosaurid</a> ankylosaurians, and titanosaurian sauropods were prevalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCL04_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-HCL04-54">[53]</a></sup> Flowering plants were greatly radiating,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-55">[54]</a></sup> with the first grasses appearing by the end of the Cretaceous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSAS05_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSAS05-58">[57]</a></sup> Grinding hadrosaurids and shearing ceratopsians became extremely diverse across North America and Asia. Theropods were also radiating as herbivores or <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Therizinosaur" title="Therizinosaur">therizinosaurians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ornithomimosauria" title="Ornithomimosauria">ornithomimosaurians</a> becoming common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, which occurred approximately 66 million years ago at the end of the Cretaceous period, caused the extinction of all dinosaur groups except for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neornithes" title="Neornithes">neornithine</a> birds. Some other <a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">diapsid</a> groups, such as crocodilians, <a href="/wiki/Sebecosuchia" title="Sebecosuchia">sebecosuchians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sphenodontia" title="Sphenodontia">sphenodontians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Choristodera" title="Choristodera">choristoderans</a>, also survived the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF04_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-AF04-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The surviving lineages of neornithine birds, including the ancestors of modern <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratites</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galloanserae" title="Galloanserae">ducks and chickens</a>, and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">waterbirds</a>, diversified rapidly at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> period, entering ecological niches left vacant by the extinction of Mesozoic dinosaur groups such as the arboreal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enantiornithine" title="Enantiornithine">enantiornithines</a>, aquatic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hesperornithine" title="Hesperornithine">hesperornithines</a>, and even the larger terrestrial theropods (in the form of <i><a href="/wiki/Gastornis" title="Gastornis">Gastornis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Eogruidae" title="Eogruidae">eogruiids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathornithids" title="Bathornithids">bathornithids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ratites" title="Ratites">ratites</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Geranoididae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Geranoididae (page does not exist)">geranoidids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mihirung" title="Mihirung">mihirungs</a>, and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terror_bird" title="Terror bird">terror birds</a>"). It is often cited that mammals out-competed the neornithines for dominance of most terrestrial niches but many of these groups co-existed with rich mammalian faunas for most of the Cenozoic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindow_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindow-60">[59]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terror_bird" title="Terror bird">Terror birds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathornithids" title="Bathornithids">bathornithids</a> occupied carnivorous guilds alongside predatory mammals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cracraft_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cracraft-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> and ratites are still fairly successful as mid-sized herbivores; eogruiids similarly lasted from the Eocene to <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a>, only becoming extinct very recently after over 20 million years of co-existence with many mammal groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_classification" title="Dinosaur classification">Dinosaur classification</a></div>
<p>Dinosaurs belong to a group known as <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a>, which also includes modern <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a>. Within the archosaur group, dinosaurs are differentiated most noticeably by their gait. Dinosaur legs extend directly beneath the body, whereas the legs of lizards and crocodilians sprawl out to either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B012_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-B012-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Collectively, dinosaurs as a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> are divided into two primary branches, <a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">Saurischia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischia</a>. Saurischia includes those taxa sharing a more recent common ancestor with birds than with <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischia</a>, while Ornithischia includes all <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a> sharing a more recent common ancestor with <i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> than with Saurischia. Anatomically, these two groups can be distinguished most noticeably by their <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvic</a> structure. Early saurischians—"lizard-hipped", from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i>sauros</i> (σαῦρος) meaning "lizard" and <i>ischion</i> (ἰσχίον) meaning "hip joint"—retained the hip structure of their ancestors, with a <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubis</a> bone directed <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">cranially</a>, or forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJB00_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-MJB00-41">[40]</a></sup> This basic form was modified by rotating the pubis backward to varying degrees in several groups (<i><a href="/wiki/Herrerasaurus" title="Herrerasaurus">Herrerasaurus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-64">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Therizinosaur" title="Therizinosaur">therizinosauroids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clarketal2004_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-clarketal2004-65">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAPM04_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAPM04-66">[65]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-56">[55]</a></sup>). Saurischia includes the <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a> (exclusively bipedal and with a wide variety of diets) and <a href="/wiki/Sauropodomorpha" title="Sauropodomorpha">sauropodomorphs</a> (long-necked <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a> which include advanced, quadrupedal groups).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theropods_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-theropods-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sauropodomorphs_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-sauropodomorphs-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, ornithischians—"bird-hipped", from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i>ornitheios</i> (ὀρνίθειος) meaning "of a bird" and <i>ischion</i> (ἰσχίον) meaning "hip joint"—had a pelvis that superficially resembled a bird's pelvis: the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubic</a> bone was oriented <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">caudally</a> (rear-pointing). Unlike birds, the ornithischian pubis also usually had an additional forward-pointing process. Ornithischia includes a variety of species which were primarily herbivores. (<b>NB:</b> the terms "lizard hip" and "bird hip" are misnomers – birds evolved from dinosaurs with "lizard hips".)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B012_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-B012-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed center">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<p>The following is a simplified classification of dinosaur groups based on their evolutionary relationships, and organized based on the list of Mesozoic dinosaur species provided by Holtz (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz2007_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holtz2007-8">[7]</a></sup> A more detailed version can be found at <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_classification" title="Dinosaur classification">Dinosaur classification</a>.
The dagger (†) is used to signify groups with no living members.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Dinosauria</b></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">Saurischia</a></b> ("lizard-hipped"; includes Theropoda and Sauropodomorpha)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Herrerasauridae" title="Herrerasauridae">Herrerasauria</a> (early bipedal carnivores)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">Theropoda</a></b> (all <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">bipedal</a>; most were carnivorous)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dromaeosaurs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="6369" height="81" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dromaeosaurs.png/220px-Dromaeosaurs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dromaeosaurs.png/330px-Dromaeosaurs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Dromaeosaurs.png/440px-Dromaeosaurs.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dromaeosaurs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of six <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurid</a> <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a>: from left to right <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Velociraptor" title="Velociraptor">Velociraptor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Austroraptor" title="Austroraptor">Austroraptor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dromaeosaurus" title="Dromaeosaurus">Dromaeosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Utahraptor" title="Utahraptor">Utahraptor</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">Deinonychus</a></i></div></div></div>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Coelophysoidea" title="Coelophysoidea">Coelophysoidea</a> (small, early theropods; includes <i><a href="/wiki/Coelophysis" title="Coelophysis">Coelophysis</a></i> and close relatives)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Dilophosauridae" title="Dilophosauridae">Dilophosauridae</a> (early crested and carnivorous theropods)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Ceratosauria" title="Ceratosauria">Ceratosauria</a> (generally elaborately horned, the dominant southern carnivores of the Cretaceous)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tetanurae" title="Tetanurae">Tetanurae</a> ("stiff tails"; includes most theropods)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Megalosauroidea" title="Megalosauroidea">Megalosauroidea</a> (early group of large carnivores including the semiaquatic spinosaurids)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">Carnosauria</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">Allosaurus</a></i> and close relatives, like <i><a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">Carcharodontosaurus</a></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">Coelurosauria</a> (feathered theropods, with a range of body sizes and niches)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-6">[5]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Compsognathidae" title="Compsognathidae">Compsognathidae</a> (common early coelurosaurs with reduced forelimbs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">Tyrannosauridae</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i> and close relatives; had reduced forelimbs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Ornithomimosauria" title="Ornithomimosauria">Ornithomimosauria</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Common_ostrich" title="Common ostrich">ostrich</a>-mimics"; mostly toothless; carnivores to possible herbivores)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alvarezsaur" title="Alvarezsaur">Alvarezsauroidea</a> (small insectivores with reduced forelimbs each bearing one enlarged claw)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">Maniraptora</a> ("hand snatchers"; had long, slender arms and fingers)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Therizinosaur" title="Therizinosaur">Therizinosauria</a> (bipedal herbivores with large hand claws and small heads)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Oviraptorosauria" title="Oviraptorosauria">Oviraptorosauria</a> (mostly toothless; their diet and lifestyle are uncertain)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Archaeopterygidae" title="Archaeopterygidae">Archaeopterygidae</a> (small, winged theropods or primitive birds)</li>
<li>†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deinonychosauria" title="Deinonychosauria">Deinonychosauria</a> (small- to medium-sized; bird-like, with a distinctive toe claw)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">Avialae</a> (modern birds and extinct relatives)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Scansoriopterygidae" title="Scansoriopterygidae">Scansoriopterygidae</a> (small primitive avialans with long third fingers)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Omnivoropterygidae" title="Omnivoropterygidae">Omnivoropterygidae</a> (large, early short-tailed avialans)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Confuciusornithidae" title="Confuciusornithidae">Confuciusornithidae</a> (small toothless avialans)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Enantiornithes" title="Enantiornithes">Enantiornithes</a> (primitive tree-dwelling, flying avialans)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Euornithes" title="Euornithes">Euornithes</a> (advanced flying birds)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Yanornithiformes" title="Yanornithiformes">Yanornithiformes</a> (toothed Cretaceous Chinese birds)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Hesperornithes" title="Hesperornithes">Hesperornithes</a> (specialized aquatic diving birds)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">Aves</a></b> (modern, beaked birds and their extinct relatives)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="1000" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg/220px-Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg/330px-Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg/440px-Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macronaria_scrubbed_enh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of four <a href="/wiki/Macronaria" title="Macronaria">macronarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">sauropods</a>: from left to right <i><a href="/wiki/Camarasaurus" title="Camarasaurus">Camarasaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Brachiosaurus" title="Brachiosaurus">Brachiosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Giraffatitan" title="Giraffatitan">Giraffatitan</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Euhelopus" title="Euhelopus">Euhelopus</a></i></div></div></div>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><ul><li>†<b><a href="/wiki/Sauropodomorpha" title="Sauropodomorpha">Sauropodomorpha</a></b> (herbivores with small heads, long necks, long tails)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Guaibasauridae" title="Guaibasauridae">Guaibasauridae</a> (small, primitive, omnivorous sauropodomorphs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Plateosauridae" title="Plateosauridae">Plateosauridae</a> (primitive, strictly bipedal "prosauropods")</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Riojasauridae" title="Riojasauridae">Riojasauridae</a> (small, primitive sauropodomorphs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Massospondylidae" title="Massospondylidae">Massospondylidae</a> (small, primitive sauropodomorphs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">Sauropoda</a> (very large and heavy, usually over 15 m (49 ft) long; quadrupedal)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Vulcanodontidae" title="Vulcanodontidae">Vulcanodontidae</a> (primitive sauropods with pillar-like limbs)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Eusauropoda" title="Eusauropoda">Eusauropoda</a> ("true sauropods")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Cetiosauridae" title="Cetiosauridae">Cetiosauridae</a> ("whale reptiles")</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Turiasauria" title="Turiasauria">Turiasauria</a> (European group of Jurassic and Cretaceous sauropods)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Neosauropoda" title="Neosauropoda">Neosauropoda</a> ("new sauropods")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Diplodocoidea" title="Diplodocoidea">Diplodocoidea</a> (skulls and tails elongated; teeth typically narrow and pencil-like)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Macronaria" title="Macronaria">Macronaria</a> (boxy skulls; spoon- or pencil-shaped teeth)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Brachiosauridae" title="Brachiosauridae">Brachiosauridae</a> (long-necked, long-armed macronarians)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Titanosaur" title="Titanosaur">Titanosauria</a> (diverse; stocky, with wide hips; most common in the late Cretaceous of southern continents)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ornithopods_jconway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="660" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ornithopods_jconway.jpg/220px-Ornithopods_jconway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ornithopods_jconway.jpg/330px-Ornithopods_jconway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ornithopods_jconway.jpg/440px-Ornithopods_jconway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ornithopods_jconway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restoration of six ornithopods; far left: <i><a href="/wiki/Camptosaurus" title="Camptosaurus">Camptosaurus</a></i>, left: <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i>, center background: <i><a href="/wiki/Shantungosaurus" title="Shantungosaurus">Shantungosaurus</a></i>, center foreground: <i><a href="/wiki/Dryosaurus" title="Dryosaurus">Dryosaurus</a></i>, right: <i><a href="/wiki/Corythosaurus" title="Corythosaurus">Corythosaurus</a></i>, far right (large) <i><a href="/wiki/Tenontosaurus" title="Tenontosaurus">Tenontosaurus</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<b><a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischia</a></b> ("bird-hipped"; diverse bipedal and quadrupedal herbivores)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Heterodontosauridae" title="Heterodontosauridae">Heterodontosauridae</a> (small basal ornithopod herbivores/omnivores with prominent <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine-like teeth</a>)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Thyreophora" title="Thyreophora">Thyreophora</a> (armored dinosaurs; mostly quadrupeds)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<b><a href="/wiki/Ankylosauria" title="Ankylosauria">Ankylosauria</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Scute" title="Scute">scutes</a> as primary armor; some had club-like tails)</li>
<li>†<b><a href="/wiki/Stegosauria" title="Stegosauria">Stegosauria</a></b> (spikes and plates as primary armor)</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Neornithischia" title="Neornithischia">Neornithischia</a> ("new ornithischians")</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<b><a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">Ornithopoda</a></b> (various sizes; bipeds and quadrupeds; evolved a method of chewing using skull flexibility and numerous teeth)</li>
<li>†<a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">Marginocephalia</a> (characterized by a cranial growth)</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>†<a href="/wiki/Pachycephalosauria" title="Pachycephalosauria">Pachycephalosauria</a> (bipeds with domed or knobby growth on skulls)</li>
<li>†<b><a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">Ceratopsia</a></b> (quadrupeds with frills; many also had horns)</li></ul></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<p>Knowledge about dinosaurs is derived from a variety of fossil and non-fossil records, including fossilized <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trackway" title="Trackway">trackways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">gastroliths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a>, impressions of skin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscus" title="Viscus">internal organs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_tissue" title="Soft tissue">soft tissues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-softtissue_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-softtissue-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzer2005_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer2005-70">[69]</a></sup> Many fields of study contribute to our understanding of dinosaurs, including <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_sciences" title="Earth sciences">earth sciences</a> (of which <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a> is a sub-discipline).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinobiomechanics_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinobiomechanics-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinobiology_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinobiology-72">[71]</a></sup> Two topics of particular interest and study have been dinosaur size and behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thedinosauria90_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-thedinosauria90-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_size" title="Dinosaur size">Dinosaur size</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="2356" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png/400px-Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png/600px-Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png/800px-Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Largestdinosaursbysuborder_scale.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scale diagram comparing the average human to the largest known dinosaurs in five major <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clades</a>: <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">Sauropoda</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus huinculensis</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">Ornithopoda</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Shantungosaurus" title="Shantungosaurus">Shantungosaurus giganteus</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">Theropoda</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Spinosaurus" title="Spinosaurus">Spinosaurus aegyptiacus</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Thyreophora" title="Thyreophora">Thyreophora</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Stegosaurus" title="Stegosaurus">Stegosaurus armatus</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">Marginocephalia</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops prorsus</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>Current evidence suggests that dinosaur average size varied through the Triassic, early Jurassic, late Jurassic and Cretaceous periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno1999_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno1999-47">[46]</a></sup> Predatory theropod dinosaurs, which occupied most terrestrial carnivore niches during the Mesozoic, most often fall into the 100 to <span style="white-space: nowrap">1<span style="margin-left: 0.25em">000</span></span> kg (220 to <span style="white-space: nowrap">2<span style="margin-left: 0.25em">200</span></span> lb) category when sorted by estimated weight into categories based on <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">order of magnitude</a>, whereas <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">recent</a> predatory <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivoran</a> mammals peak in the 10 to 100 kg (22 to 220 lb) category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF93_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-JF93-74">[73]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mode_(statistics)" title="Mode (statistics)">mode</a> of Mesozoic dinosaur body masses is between one and ten metric tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peczkis1994_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peczkis1994-75">[74]</a></sup> This contrasts sharply with the average size of <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> mammals, estimated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum of Natural History</a> as about 2 to 5 kg (4.4 to 11.0 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMNH_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMNH-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">sauropods</a> were the largest and heaviest dinosaurs. For much of the dinosaur era, the smallest sauropods were larger than anything else in their habitat, and the largest were an <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">order of magnitude</a> more massive than anything else that has since walked the Earth. Giant prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Paraceratherium" title="Paraceratherium">Paraceratherium</a></i> (the largest land mammal ever) were dwarfed by the giant sauropods, and only modern whales approach or surpass them in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> There are several proposed advantages for the large size of sauropods, including protection from predation, reduction of energy use, and longevity, but it may be that the most important advantage was dietary. Large animals are more efficient at digestion than small animals, because food spends more time in their digestive systems. This also permits them to subsist on food with lower nutritive value than smaller animals. Sauropod remains are mostly found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_formation" title="Rock formation">rock formations</a> interpreted as dry or seasonally dry, and the ability to eat large quantities of low-nutrient browse would have been advantageous in such environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_and_smallest">Largest and smallest</span></h4>
<p>Scientists will probably never be certain of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Largest_organism" title="Largest organism">largest and smallest dinosaurs</a> to have ever existed. This is because only a tiny percentage of animals ever fossilize, and most of these remain buried in the earth. Few of the specimens that are recovered are complete skeletons, and impressions of skin and other soft tissues are rare. Rebuilding a complete skeleton by comparing the size and morphology of bones to those of similar, better-known species is an inexact art, and reconstructing the muscles and other organs of the living animal is, at best, a process of educated guesswork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP10_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP10-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Argentinosaurus_9.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="800" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Argentinosaurus_9.svg/220px-Argentinosaurus_9.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Argentinosaurus_9.svg/330px-Argentinosaurus_9.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Argentinosaurus_9.svg/440px-Argentinosaurus_9.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Argentinosaurus_9.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative size of <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i> to the average human</div></div></div>
<p>The tallest and heaviest dinosaur known from good skeletons is <i><a href="/wiki/Giraffatitan" title="Giraffatitan">Giraffatitan brancai</a></i> (previously classified as a species of <i><a href="/wiki/Brachiosaurus" title="Brachiosaurus">Brachiosaurus</a></i>). Its remains were discovered in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> between 1907 and 1912. Bones from several similar-sized individuals were incorporated into the skeleton now mounted and on display at the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_Berlin" title="Natural History Museum, Berlin">Museum für Naturkunde Berlin</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC68_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-EC68-79">[78]</a></sup> this mount is 12 meters (39 ft) tall and 21.8–22.5 meters (72–74 ft) long,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> and would have belonged to an animal that weighed between <span style="white-space:nowrap">30<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap">60<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> kilograms (<span style="white-space:nowrap">70<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap">130<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> lb). The longest complete dinosaur is the 27 meters (89 feet) long <i><a href="/wiki/Diplodocus" title="Diplodocus">Diplodocus</a></i>, which was discovered in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and displayed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania" title="Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania">Pittsburgh's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnegie_Natural_History_Museum" title="Carnegie Natural History Museum">Carnegie Natural History Museum</a> in 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hecket04_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-hecket04-82">[81]</a></sup> The longest dinosaur known from good fossil material is the <i><a href="/wiki/Patagotitan" title="Patagotitan">Patagotitan</a></i>: the skeleton mount in the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> is 37 meters (121 ft) long. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carmen_Funes_Museum" title="Carmen Funes Museum">Carmen Funes Museum</a> has an <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i> reconstructed skeleton mount 39.7 metres (130 ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plosone_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-plosone-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
