<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human-eoraptor_size_comparison(v2).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="460" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Human-eoraptor_size_comparison%28v2%29.png/220px-Human-eoraptor_size_comparison%28v2%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Human-eoraptor_size_comparison%28v2%29.png/330px-Human-eoraptor_size_comparison%28v2%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Human-eoraptor_size_comparison%28v2%29.png/440px-Human-eoraptor_size_comparison%28v2%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human-eoraptor_size_comparison(v2).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative size of <i><a href="/wiki/Eoraptor" title="Eoraptor">Eoraptor</a></i> to the average human</div></div></div>
<p>There were larger dinosaurs, but knowledge of them is based entirely on a small number of fragmentary fossils. Most of the largest <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a> specimens on record were discovered in the 1970s or later, and include the massive <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i>, which may have weighed <span style="white-space:nowrap">80<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> to <span style="white-space:nowrap">100<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> kilograms (90 to 110 short tons) and reached length of 30–40 metres (98–131 ft); some of the longest were the 33.5 meters (110 ft) long <i><a href="/wiki/Diplodocus" title="Diplodocus">Diplodocus hallorum</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-15">[14]</a></sup> (formerly <i>Seismosaurus</i>), the 33–34 meters (108–112 ft) long <i><a href="/wiki/Supersaurus" title="Supersaurus">Supersaurus</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LHW07_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-LHW07-84">[83]</a></sup> and 37 metres (121 ft) long <i><a href="/wiki/Patagotitan" title="Patagotitan">Patagotitan</a></i>; and the tallest, the 18 meters (59 ft) tall <i><a href="/wiki/Sauroposeidon" title="Sauroposeidon">Sauroposeidon</a></i>, which could have reached a sixth-floor window. The heaviest and longest dinosaur may have been <i><a href="/wiki/Amphicoelias" title="Amphicoelias">Amphicoelias fragillimus</a></i>, known only from a now lost partial vertebral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_arch" title="Neural arch">neural arch</a> described in 1878. Extrapolating from the illustration of this bone, the animal may have been 58 meters (190 ft) long and weighed <span style="white-space:nowrap">122<span style="margin-left:0.25em">400</span></span> kg (<span style="white-space:nowrap">270<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-15">[14]</a></sup> However, as no further evidence of sauropods of this size has been found, and the discoverer, Edward Cope, had made typographic errors before, it is likely to have been an extreme overestimation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> As of 2018, <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Patagotitan" title="Patagotitan">Patagotitan</a></i> are considered by paleontologists as the largest dinosaurs known from reasonable remains.
</p><p>The largest <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivorous</a> dinosaur was <i><a href="/wiki/Spinosaurus" title="Spinosaurus">Spinosaurus</a></i>, reaching a length of 12.6 to 18 meters (41 to 59 ft), and weighing 7–20.9 tonnes (7.7–23 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMBM06_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMBM06-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-TH07-87">[86]</a></sup> Other large carnivorous theropods included <i><a href="/wiki/Giganotosaurus" title="Giganotosaurus">Giganotosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">Carcharodontosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-TH07-87">[86]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Therizinosaurus" title="Therizinosaurus">Therizinosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Deinocheirus" title="Deinocheirus">Deinocheirus</a></i> were among the tallest of the theropods. The largest <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">Ornithischian</a> dinosaur was probably the <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurid" title="Hadrosaurid">hadrosaurid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Shantungosaurus" title="Shantungosaurus">Shantungosaurus</a></i> which measured 16 metres (52 ft) and weighed about 13 tonnes (29,000 lb).
</p><p>The smallest dinosaur known is the <a href="/wiki/Bee_hummingbird" title="Bee hummingbird">bee hummingbird</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovering_dinosaurs_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-discovering_dinosaurs-88">[87]</a></sup> with a length of only 5 cm (2.0 in) and mass of around 1.8 g (0.063 oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> The smallest known non-<a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">avialan</a> dinosaurs were about the size of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> and were those theropods most closely related to birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhang2008_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhang2008-90">[89]</a></sup> For example, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchiornis_huxleyi" title="Anchiornis huxleyi">Anchiornis huxleyi</a></i> is currently the smallest non-avialan dinosaur described from an adult specimen, with an estimated weight of 110 grams<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anchiadvance_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-anchiadvance-91">[90]</a></sup> and a total skeletal length of 34 cm (1.12 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhang2008_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-zhang2008-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anchiadvance_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-anchiadvance-91">[90]</a></sup> The smallest herbivorous non-avialan dinosaurs included <i><a href="/wiki/Microceratus" title="Microceratus">Microceratus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wannanosaurus" title="Wannanosaurus">Wannanosaurus</a></i>, at about 60 cm (2.0 ft) long each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz2007_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holtz2007-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-butler&amp;zhao2009_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-butler&amp;zhao2009-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maiasaurusnest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Maiasaurusnest.jpg/220px-Maiasaurusnest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Maiasaurusnest.jpg/330px-Maiasaurusnest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Maiasaurusnest.jpg/440px-Maiasaurusnest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maiasaurusnest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A nesting ground of hadrosaur <i><a href="/wiki/Maiasaura" title="Maiasaura">Maiasaura peeblesorum</a></i> was discovered in 1978.</div></div></div>
<p>Many modern birds are highly social, often found living in flocks. There is general agreement that some behaviors that are common in birds, as well as in crocodiles (birds' closest living relatives), were also common among extinct dinosaur groups. Interpretations of behavior in fossil species are generally based on the pose of skeletons and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulations</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a>, and comparisons with modern animals in similar <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinobiomechanics_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-dinobiomechanics-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first potential evidence for <a href="/wiki/Herd" title="Herd">herding</a> or <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">flocking</a> as a widespread behavior common to many dinosaur groups in addition to birds was the 1878 discovery of 31 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iguanodon_bernissartensis" title="Iguanodon bernissartensis">Iguanodon bernissartensis</a></i>, ornithischians that were then thought to have perished together in <a href="/wiki/Bernissart" title="Bernissart">Bernissart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, after they fell into a deep, flooded <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkhole</a> and drowned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yans_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yans-93">[92]</a></sup> Other mass-death sites have been discovered subsequently. Those, along with multiple trackways, suggest that gregarious behavior was common in many early dinosaur species. Trackways of hundreds or even thousands of herbivores indicate that <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurid" title="Hadrosaurid">duck-bills</a> (hadrosaurids) may have moved in great herds, like the <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">American bison</a> or the African <a href="/wiki/Springbok" title="Springbok">Springbok</a>. Sauropod tracks document that these animals traveled in groups composed of several different species, at least in <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">Oxfordshire</a>, England,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day2002_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day2002-94">[93]</a></sup> although there is no evidence for specific herd structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JLW05_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-JLW05-95">[94]</a></sup> Congregating into herds may have evolved for defense, for <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> purposes, or to provide protection for young. There is evidence that many types of slow-growing dinosaurs, including various theropods, sauropods, ankylosaurians, ornithopods, and ceratopsians, formed aggregations of immature individuals. One example is a site in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> that has yielded the remains of over 20 <i><a href="/wiki/Sinornithomimus" title="Sinornithomimus">Sinornithomimus</a></i>, from one to seven years old. This assemblage is interpreted as a social group that was trapped in mud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DVetal08sino_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-DVetal08sino-96">[95]</a></sup> The interpretation of dinosaurs as gregarious has also extended to depicting carnivorous theropods as <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">pack hunters</a> working together to bring down large prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG93_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-LG93-97">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxwell&amp;ostrom1995_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-maxwell&amp;ostrom1995-98">[97]</a></sup> However, this lifestyle is uncommon among modern birds, <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, and other reptiles, and the <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomic</a> evidence suggesting mammal-like pack hunting in such theropods as <i><a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">Deinonychus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">Allosaurus</a></i> can also be interpreted as the results of fatal disputes between feeding animals, as is seen in many modern <a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">diapsid</a> predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RB07_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-RB07-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="808" data-file-width="1700" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png/220px-Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png/330px-Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png/440px-Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Centrosaurus_dinosaur.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's rendering of two <i><a href="/wiki/Centrosaurus" title="Centrosaurus">Centrosaurus apertus</a></i> engaged in intra-specific combat</div></div></div>
<p>The crests and <a href="/wiki/Neck_frill" title="Neck frill">frills</a> of some dinosaurs, like the <a href="/wiki/Marginocephalia" title="Marginocephalia">marginocephalians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambeosaurine" title="Lambeosaurine">lambeosaurines</a>, may have been too fragile to be used for active defense, and so they were likely used for sexual or aggressive displays, though little is known about dinosaur mating and <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorialism</a>. Head wounds from bites suggest that theropods, at least, engaged in active aggressive confrontations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PC98_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-PC98-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>From a behavioral standpoint, one of the most valuable dinosaur fossils was discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a> in 1971. It included a <i><a href="/wiki/Velociraptor" title="Velociraptor">Velociraptor</a></i> attacking a <i><a href="/wiki/Protoceratops" title="Protoceratops">Protoceratops</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMNH_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMNH-101">[100]</a></sup> providing evidence that dinosaurs did indeed attack each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1998_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-carpenter1998-102">[101]</a></sup> Additional evidence for attacking live prey is the partially healed tail of an <i><a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurus" title="Edmontosaurus">Edmontosaurus</a></i>, a hadrosaurid dinosaur; the tail is damaged in such a way that shows the animal was bitten by a tyrannosaur but survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter1998_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-carpenter1998-102">[101]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">Cannibalism</a> amongst some species of dinosaurs was confirmed by tooth marks found in Madagascar in 2003, involving the theropod <i><a href="/wiki/Majungasaurus" title="Majungasaurus">Majungasaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogersetal2003_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-rogersetal2003-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comparisons between the <a href="/wiki/Sclerotic_ring" title="Sclerotic ring">scleral rings</a> of dinosaurs and modern birds and reptiles have been used to infer daily activity patterns of dinosaurs. Although it has been suggested that most dinosaurs were active during the day, these comparisons have shown that small predatory dinosaurs such as <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurids</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Juravenator" title="Juravenator">Juravenator</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megapnosaurus" title="Megapnosaurus">Megapnosaurus</a></i> were likely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a>. Large and medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivorous</a> dinosaurs such as <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">ceratopsians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauropodomorpha" title="Sauropodomorpha">sauropodomorphs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrosauridae" title="Hadrosauridae">hadrosaurids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ornithomimosauria" title="Ornithomimosauria">ornithomimosaurs</a> may have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathemeral" title="Cathemeral">cathemeral</a>, active during short intervals throughout the day, although the small <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">ornithischian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Agilisaurus" title="Agilisaurus">Agilisaurus</a></i> was inferred to be <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchmitzMotani2011_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchmitzMotani2011-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on current fossil evidence from dinosaurs such as <i><a href="/wiki/Oryctodromeus" title="Oryctodromeus">Oryctodromeus</a></i>, some ornithischian species seem to have led a partially <a href="/wiki/Fossorial" title="Fossorial">fossorial</a> (burrowing) lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VMK07_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-VMK07-105">[104]</a></sup> Many modern birds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboreal" title="Arboreal">arboreal</a> (tree climbing), and this was also true of many Mesozoic birds, especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enantiornithine" title="Enantiornithine">enantiornithines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> While some early bird-like species may have already been arboreal as well (including <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">dromaeosaurids</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chatterjee2007_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-chatterjee2007-107">[106]</a></sup>) most non-avialan dinosaurs seem to have relied on land-based locomotion. A good understanding of how dinosaurs moved on the ground is key to models of dinosaur behavior; the science of <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a>, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNeill_Alexander" title="Robert McNeill Alexander">Robert McNeill Alexander</a>, has provided significant insight in this area. For example, studies of the forces exerted by muscles and gravity on dinosaurs' skeletal structure have investigated how fast dinosaurs could run,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alexander2006_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-alexander2006-108">[107]</a></sup> whether <a href="/wiki/Diplodocid" title="Diplodocid">diplodocids</a> could create <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">sonic booms</a> via <a href="/wiki/Whip" title="Whip">whip</a>-like tail snapping,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goriely_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-goriely-109">[108]</a></sup> and whether sauropods could float.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson2006_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson2006-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="761" data-file-width="900" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg/220px-Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg/330px-Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg/440px-Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lambeosaurus_magnicristatus_DB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a striking and unusual visual display in a <i><a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurus" title="Lambeosaurus">Lambeosaurus magnicristatus</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Modern birds are known to <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">communicate</a> using visual and auditory signals, and the wide diversity of visual display structures among fossil dinosaur groups, such as horns, frills, crests, sails and feathers, suggests that visual communication has always been important in dinosaur biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2008_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-senter2008-111">[110]</a></sup> Reconstruction of the plumage color of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchiornis_huxleyi" title="Anchiornis huxleyi">Anchiornis huxleyi</a></i>, suggest the importance of color in visual communication in non-avian dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2010_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li2010-112">[111]</a></sup> The evolution of dinosaur vocalization is less certain. Paleontologist Phil Senter suggests that non-avian dinosaurs relied mostly on visual displays and possibly non-vocal acoustic sounds like hissing, jaw grinding or clapping, splashing and wing beating (possible in winged <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">maniraptoran</a> dinosaurs). He states they were unlikely to have been capable of vocalizing since their closest relatives, crocodilians and birds, use different means to vocalize, the former via the <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> and the latter through the unique <a href="/wiki/Syrinx_(bird_anatomy)" title="Syrinx (bird anatomy)">syrinx</a>, suggesting they evolved independently and their common ancestor was mute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2008_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-senter2008-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest remains of a syrinx, which has enough mineral content for fossilization, was found in a specimen of the <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">duck</a>-like <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegavis_iaai" title="Vegavis iaai">Vegavis iaai</a></i> dated 69-66 million year ago, and this organ is unlikely to have existed in non-avian dinosaurs. However, in contrast to Senter, the researchers have suggested that dinosaurs could vocalize and that the syrinx-based vocal system of birds evolved from a larynx-based one, rather than the two systems evolving independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke2016_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke2016-113">[112]</a></sup> A 2016 study suggests that dinosaurs produced closed mouth vocalizations like cooing, which occur in both crocodilians and birds as well as other reptiles. Such vocalizations evolved independently in extant archosaurs numerous times, following increases in body size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobias2016_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobias2016-114">[113]</a></sup> The crests of the <a href="/wiki/Lambeosaurini" title="Lambeosaurini">Lambeosaurini</a> and nasal chambers of ankylosaurids have been suggested to function in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vocal_resonance" title="Vocal resonance">vocal resonance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weishampel981_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weishampel981-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witmer_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witmer-116">[115]</a></sup> though Senter states that the presence of resonance chambers in some dinosaurs is not necessarily evidence of vocalization as modern snakes have such chambers which intensify their hisses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2008_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-senter2008-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_biology">Reproductive biology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_egg" title="Dinosaur egg">Dinosaur egg</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG"><img alt="Three bluish eggs with black speckling sit atop a layer of white mollusk shell pieces, surrounded by sandy ground and small bits of bluish stone" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG/220px-Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG/330px-Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG/440px-Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gniazdo_sieweczki_RB.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nest of a <a href="/wiki/Plover" title="Plover">plover</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Charadrius" title="Charadrius">Charadrius</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>All dinosaurs lay <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amniotic_egg" title="Amniotic egg">amniotic eggs</a> with hard shells made mostly of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Eggs are usually laid in a <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nest</a>. Most species create somewhat elaborate nests, which can be cups, domes, plates, beds scrapes, mounds, or burrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansell_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansell-118">[117]</a></sup> Some species of modern bird have no nests; the cliff-nesting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_guillemot" title="Common guillemot">common guillemot</a> lays its eggs on bare rock, and male <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">emperor penguins</a> keep eggs between their body and feet. Primitive birds and many non-avialan dinosaurs often lay eggs in communal nests, with males primarily incubating the eggs. While modern birds have only one functional oviduct and lay one egg at a time, more primitive birds and dinosaurs had two oviducts, like crocodiles. Some non-avialan dinosaurs, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Troodon" title="Troodon">Troodon</a></i>, exhibited iterative laying, where the adult might lay a pair of eggs every one or two days, and then ensured simultaneous hatching by delaying brooding until all eggs were laid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varrichioetal.02_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varrichioetal.02-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>When laying eggs, females grow a special type of bone between the hard outer bone and the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">marrow</a> of their limbs. This medullary bone, which is rich in <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, is used to make eggshells. A discovery of features in a <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus rex</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a> provided evidence of medullary bone in extinct dinosaurs and, for the first time, allowed paleontologists to establish the sex of a fossil dinosaur specimen. Further research has found medullary bone in the carnosaur <i><a href="/wiki/Allosaurus" title="Allosaurus">Allosaurus</a></i> and the ornithopod <i><a href="/wiki/Tenontosaurus" title="Tenontosaurus">Tenontosaurus</a></i>. Because the line of dinosaurs that includes <i>Allosaurus</i> and <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> diverged from the line that led to <i>Tenontosaurus</i> very early in the evolution of dinosaurs, this suggests that the production of medullary tissue is a general characteristic of all dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LW08_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-LW08-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg/220px-Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg/330px-Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg/440px-Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Citipati_IGM_100_979.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fossil interpreted as a nesting <a href="/wiki/Oviraptoridae" title="Oviraptoridae">oviraptorid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Citipati" title="Citipati">Citipati</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>. Smaller fossil far right showing inside one of the eggs.</div></div></div>
<p>Another widespread trait among modern birds (but see below in regards to fossil groups and extant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megapodes" title="Megapodes">megapodes</a>) is parental care for young after hatching. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Horner_(paleontologist)" title="Jack Horner (paleontologist)">Jack Horner's</a> 1978 discovery of a <i><a href="/wiki/Maiasaura" title="Maiasaura">Maiasaura</a></i> ("good mother lizard") <a href="/wiki/Nest" title="Nest">nesting</a> ground in <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a> demonstrated that parental care continued long after birth among <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">ornithopods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HM79_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-HM79-121">[120]</a></sup> A specimen of the <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolian</a> <a href="/wiki/Oviraptoridae" title="Oviraptoridae">oviraptorid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Citipati" title="Citipati">Citipati osmolskae</a></i> was discovered in a <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a>-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" title="Avian incubation">brooding</a> position in 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-search.eb_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-search.eb-122">[121]</a></sup> which may indicate that they had begun using an insulating layer of feathers to keep the eggs warm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> A dinosaur embryo (pertaining to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosauropoda" title="Prosauropoda">prosauropod</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Massospondylus" title="Massospondylus">Massospondylus</a></i>) was found without teeth, indicating that some parental care was required to feed the young dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reiszetal05_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reiszetal05-124">[123]</a></sup> Trackways have also confirmed parental behavior among ornithopods from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" title="Isle of Skye">Isle of Skye</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCtracks_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCtracks-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, there is ample evidence of supreprecociality among many dinosaur species, particularly theropods. For instance, non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithuromorph" title="Ornithuromorph">ornithuromorph</a> birds have been abundantly demonstrated to have had slow growth rates, <a href="/wiki/Megapode" title="Megapode">megapode</a>-like egg burying behaviour and the ability to fly soon after birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> Both <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus_rex" title="Tyrannosaurus rex">Tyrannosaurus rex</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troodon_formosus" title="Troodon formosus">Troodon formosus</a></i> display juveniles with clear supreprecociality and likely occupying different ecological niches than the adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> Superprecociality has been inferred for sauropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physiology_of_dinosaurs" title="Physiology of dinosaurs">Physiology of dinosaurs</a></div>
<p>Because both modern <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> have four-chambered hearts (albeit modified in crocodilians), it is likely that this is a trait shared by all archosaurs, including all dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CH04_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-CH04-131">[130]</a></sup> While all modern birds have high metabolisms and are "warm blooded" (endothermic), a vigorous debate has been ongoing since the 1960s regarding how far back in the dinosaur lineage this trait extends. Scientists disagree as to whether non-avian dinosaurs were endothermic, ectothermic, or some combination of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>After non-avian dinosaurs were discovered, paleontologists first posited that they were <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectothermic</a>. This supposed "cold-bloodedness" was used to imply that the ancient dinosaurs were relatively slow, sluggish organisms, even though many modern reptiles are fast and light-footed despite relying on external sources of heat to regulate their body temperature. The idea of dinosaurs as ectothermic and sluggish remained a prevalent view until <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">Robert T. "Bob" Bakker</a>, an early proponent of dinosaur endothermy, published an influential paper on the topic in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern evidence indicates that even non-avian dinosaurs and birds thrived in cooler temperate climates, and that at least some early species must have regulated their body temperature by internal biological means (aided by the animals' bulk in large species and feathers or other body coverings in smaller species). Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">endothermy</a> in Mesozoic dinosaurs includes the discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_dinosaurs_in_Australia" title="Polar dinosaurs in Australia">polar dinosaurs in Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> as well as analysis of blood-vessel structures within fossil bones that are typical of endotherms. Scientific debate continues regarding the specific ways in which dinosaur temperature regulation evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parsons2001_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-parsons2001-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dino_temp_2015_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-dino_temp_2015-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dino_bird_h.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="999" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Dino_bird_h.jpg/220px-Dino_bird_h.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Dino_bird_h.jpg/330px-Dino_bird_h.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Dino_bird_h.jpg/440px-Dino_bird_h.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dino_bird_h.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_sacs" title="Air sacs">air sacs</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Abelisaur" title="Abelisaur">abelisaur</a> and a bird</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Saurischia" title="Saurischia">saurischian</a> dinosaurs, higher metabolisms were supported by the evolution of the avian respiratory system, characterized by an extensive system of air sacs that extended the lungs and invaded many of the bones in the skeleton, making them hollow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerenoMartinezEtAl2008AvianIntrathoracicAirSacsInNewPredatoryDinosaur_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-SerenoMartinezEtAl2008AvianIntrathoracicAirSacsInNewPredatoryDinosaur-136">[135]</a></sup> Early avian-style respiratory systems with air sacs may have been capable of sustaining higher activity levels than those of mammals of similar size and build. In addition to providing a very efficient supply of oxygen, the rapid airflow would have been an effective cooling mechanism, which is essential for animals that are active but too large to get rid of all the excess heat through their skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReidInFarlowBrettSurman1997_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReidInFarlowBrettSurman1997-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Like other <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, dinosaurs are primarily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uricotelic" title="Uricotelic">uricotelic</a>, that is, their <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> extract nitrogenous wastes from their bloodstream and excrete it as <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> via the ureters into the intestine. In most living species, uric acid is excreted along with feces as a semisolid waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coprodeum_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-coprodeum-140">[139]</a></sup> However, at least some modern birds (such as <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a>) can be facultatively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonotelic" title="Ammonotelic">ammonotelic</a>, excreting most of the nitrogenous wastes as ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> They also excrete <a href="/wiki/Creatine" title="Creatine">creatine</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Creatinine" title="Creatinine">creatinine</a> like mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> This material, as well as the output of the intestines, emerges from the <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloaca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> In addition, many species regurgitate <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">pellets</a>, and fossil pellets that may have come from dinosaurs are known from as long ago as the Cretaceous period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_birds">Origin of birds</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_birds" title="Origin of birds">Origin of birds</a></div>
<p>The possibility that dinosaurs were the ancestors of birds was first suggested in 1868 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huxley1868_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-huxley1868-146">[145]</a></sup> After the work of <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Heilmann" title="Gerhard Heilmann">Gerhard Heilmann</a> in the early 20th century, the theory of birds as dinosaur descendants was abandoned in favor of the idea of their being descendants of generalized <a href="/wiki/Thecodontia" title="Thecodontia">thecodonts</a>, with the key piece of evidence being the supposed lack of <a href="/wiki/Clavicle" title="Clavicle">clavicles</a> in dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heilmann_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-heilmann-147">[146]</a></sup> However, as later discoveries showed, clavicles (or a single fused <a href="/wiki/Furcula" title="Furcula">wishbone</a>, which derived from separate clavicles) were not actually absent;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-56">[55]</a></sup> they had been found as early as 1924 in <i>Oviraptor</i>, but misidentified as an <a href="/wiki/Interclavicle" title="Interclavicle">interclavicle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HO24_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-HO24-148">[147]</a></sup> In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/John_Ostrom" title="John Ostrom">John Ostrom</a> revived the dinosaur–bird theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ostrom1973_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-ostrom1973-149">[148]</a></sup> which gained momentum in the coming decades with the advent of cladistic analysis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauthier1986_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-gauthier1986-150">[149]</a></sup> and a great increase in the discovery of small theropods and early birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRHJ00_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-TRHJ00-36">[35]</a></sup> Of particular note have been the fossils of the <a href="/wiki/Yixian_Formation" title="Yixian Formation">Yixian Formation</a>, where a variety of theropods and early birds have been found, often with feathers of some type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-56">[55]</a></sup> Birds share over a hundred distinct anatomical features with theropod dinosaurs, which are now generally accepted to have been their closest ancient relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayretal2005_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayretal2005-151">[150]</a></sup>
They are most closely allied with <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">maniraptoran</a> coelurosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-56">[55]</a></sup> A minority of scientists, most notably <a href="/wiki/Alan_Feduccia" title="Alan Feduccia">Alan Feduccia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larry_Martin" title="Larry Martin">Larry Martin</a>, have proposed other evolutionary paths, including revised versions of Heilmann's basal archosaur proposal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martin2004_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-martin2004-152">[151]</a></sup> or that maniraptoran theropods are the ancestors of birds but themselves are not dinosaurs, only <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent</a> with dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF02_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-AF02-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feathers">Feathers</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaurs" title="Feathered dinosaurs">Feathered dinosaurs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2444" data-file-width="2372" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg/220px-Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg/330px-Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg/440px-Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feathered_non-avian_Maniraptora.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various feathered non-avian dinosaurs, including <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">Archaeopteryx</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Anchiornis" title="Anchiornis">Anchiornis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">Microraptor</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zhenyuanlong" title="Zhenyuanlong">Zhenyuanlong</a></i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">Feathers</a> are one of the most recognizable characteristics of modern birds, and a trait that was shared by all other dinosaur groups. Based on the current distribution of fossil evidence, it appears that feathers were an ancestral dinosaurian trait, though one that may have been selectively lost in some species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switeknature_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-switeknature-154">[153]</a></sup> Direct fossil evidence of feathers or feather-like structures has been discovered in a diverse array of species in many non-avian dinosaur groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-6">[5]</a></sup> both among saurischians and ornithischians. Simple, branched, feather-like structures are known from <a href="/wiki/Heterodontosauridae" title="Heterodontosauridae">heterodontosaurids</a>, primitive <a href="/wiki/Neornithischia" title="Neornithischia">neornithischians</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-svp2013abs_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-svp2013abs-155">[154]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xuetal2004_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xuetal2004-156">[155]</a></sup> and primitive <a href="/wiki/Ceratopsia" title="Ceratopsia">ceratopsians</a>. Evidence for true, vaned feathers similar to the flight feathers of modern birds has been found only in the theropod subgroup <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">Maniraptora</a>, which includes oviraptorosaurs, troodontids, dromaeosaurids, and birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KP04_56-5"><a href="#cite_note-KP04-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GC06_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-GC06-157">[156]</a></sup> Feather-like structures known as pycnofibres have also been found in <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kellneretal2009_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-kellneretal2009-158">[157]</a></sup> suggesting the possibility that feather-like filaments may have been common in the <a href="/wiki/Avemetatarsalia" title="Avemetatarsalia">bird lineage</a> and evolved before the appearance of dinosaurs themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switeknature_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-switeknature-154">[153]</a></sup> Research into the genetics of <a href="/wiki/American_alligator" title="American alligator">American alligators</a> has also revealed that crocodylian <a href="/wiki/Scute" title="Scute">scutes</a> do possess feather-keratins during embryonic development, but these keratins are not expressed by the animals before hatching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlibL2006_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlibL2006-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">Archaeopteryx</a></i> was the first fossil found that revealed a potential connection between dinosaurs and birds. It is considered a <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">transitional fossil</a>, in that it displays features of both groups. Brought to light just two years after Darwin's seminal <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">The Origin of Species</a></i>, its discovery spurred the nascent debate between proponents of <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">creationism</a>. This early bird is so dinosaur-like that, without a clear impression of feathers in the surrounding rock, at least one specimen was mistaken for <i><a href="/wiki/Compsognathus" title="Compsognathus">Compsognathus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PW88_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-PW88-160">[159]</a></sup> Since the 1990s, a number of additional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaurs" title="Feathered dinosaurs">feathered dinosaurs</a> have been found, providing even stronger evidence of the close relationship between dinosaurs and modern birds. Most of these specimens were unearthed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagerst%C3%A4tten" title="Lagerstätten">lagerstätte</a> of the Yixian Formation, <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">Liaoning</a>, northeastern China, which was part of an island continent during the Cretaceous. Though feathers have been found in only a few locations, it is possible that non-avian dinosaurs elsewhere in the world were also feathered. The lack of widespread fossil evidence for feathered non-avian dinosaurs may be because delicate features like skin and feathers are not often preserved by fossilization and thus are absent from the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzeretal1999_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzeretal1999-161">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>The description of feathered dinosaurs has not been without controversy; perhaps the most vocal critics have been Alan Feduccia and Theagarten Lingham-Soliar, who have proposed that some purported feather-like fossils are the result of the decomposition of collagenous fiber that underlaid the dinosaurs' skin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TLS03_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-TLS03-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLH05_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FLH05-163">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSFX07_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSFX07-164">[163]</a></sup> and that maniraptoran dinosaurs with vaned feathers were not actually dinosaurs, but <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent</a> with dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF02_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-AF02-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLH05_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FLH05-163">[162]</a></sup> However, their views have for the most part not been accepted by other researchers, to the point that the scientific nature of Feduccia's proposals has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prum2003_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prum2003-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, it was reported that a dinosaur tail with feathers had been found enclosed in <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>. The fossil is about 99 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skeleton">Skeleton</span></h3>
<p>Because feathers are often associated with birds, feathered dinosaurs are often touted as the <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">missing link</a> between birds and dinosaurs. However, the multiple skeletal features also shared by the two groups represent another important line of evidence for paleontologists. Areas of the skeleton with important similarities include the neck, <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wrist" title="Wrist">wrist</a> (semi-lunate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpal" title="Carpal">carpal</a>), arm and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pectoral_girdle" title="Pectoral girdle">pectoral girdle</a>, furcula (wishbone), and <a href="/wiki/Keel_(bird_anatomy)" title="Keel (bird anatomy)">breast bone</a>. Comparison of bird and dinosaur skeletons through cladistic analysis strengthens the case for the link.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archaeopteryxucmp_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-archaeopteryxucmp-168">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_anatomy">Soft anatomy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2342" data-file-width="1876" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg/220px-Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg/330px-Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg/440px-Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pneumatopores_on_the_left_ilium_of_the_theropod_Aerosteon_riocoloradensis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pneumatopores on the left <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Aerosteon" title="Aerosteon">Aerosteon riocoloradensis</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Large meat-eating dinosaurs had a complex system of air sacs similar to those found in modern birds, according to a 2005 investigation led by Patrick M. O'Connor. The lungs of theropod dinosaurs (carnivores that walked on two legs and had bird-like feet) likely pumped air into hollow sacs in their <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a>, as is the case in birds. "What was once formally considered unique to birds was present in some form in the ancestors of birds", O'Connor said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConnorClaessens2005_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-OConnorClaessens2005-169">[168]</a></sup> In 2008, scientists described <i><a href="/wiki/Aerosteon" title="Aerosteon">Aerosteon riocoloradensis</a></i>, the skeleton of which supplies the strongest evidence to date of a dinosaur with a bird-like breathing system. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computed_tomography" title="Computed tomography">CT-scanning</a> of <i>Aerosteon'</i>s fossil bones revealed evidence for the existence of air sacs within the animal's body cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sereno2008_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sereno2008-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newswise2_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-newswise2-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_evidence">Behavioral evidence</span></h3>
<p>Fossils of the <a href="/wiki/Troodontidae" title="Troodontidae">troodonts</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mei_(dinosaur)" title="Mei (dinosaur)">Mei</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sinornithoides" title="Sinornithoides">Sinornithoides</a></i> demonstrate that some dinosaurs slept with their heads tucked under their arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XUNorell2004_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-XUNorell2004-172">[171]</a></sup> This behavior, which may have helped to keep the head warm, is also characteristic of modern birds. Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deinonychosauria" title="Deinonychosauria">deinonychosaur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oviraptorosauria" title="Oviraptorosauria">oviraptorosaur</a> specimens have also been found preserved on top of their nests, likely brooding in a bird-like manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norell1995_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-norell1995-173">[172]</a></sup> The ratio between egg volume and body mass of adults among these dinosaurs suggest that the eggs were primarily brooded by the male, and that the young were highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precocial" title="Precocial">precocial</a>, similar to many modern ground-dwelling birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varricchioetal2008_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varricchioetal2008-174">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some dinosaurs are known to have used <a href="/wiki/Gizzard" title="Gizzard">gizzard</a> stones like modern birds. These stones are swallowed by animals to aid digestion and break down food and hard fibers once they enter the stomach. When found in association with fossils, gizzard stones are called <a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">gastroliths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wings2007_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-wings2007-175">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinction_of_major_groups">Extinction of major groups</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a></div>
<p>The discovery that birds are a type of dinosaur showed that dinosaurs in general are not, in fact, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a> as is commonly stated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mistaken_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-mistaken-176">[175]</a></sup> However, all non-avian dinosaurs, estimated to have been 628-1078 species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> as well as many groups of birds did suddenly become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a> approximately 66 million years ago. It has been suggested that because small mammals, <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">squamata</a> and birds occupied the ecological niches suited for small body size, non-avian dinosaurs never evolved a diverse fauna of small-bodied species, which led to their downfall when large-bodied terrestrial tetrapods were hit by the mass extinction event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> Many other groups of animals also became extinct at this time, including <a href="/wiki/Ammonoidea" title="Ammonoidea">ammonites</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautilus</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusks</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur">mosasaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plesiosauria" title="Plesiosauria">plesiosaurs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pterosauria" title="Pterosauria">pterosaurs</a>, and many groups of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-13">[12]</a></sup> Significantly, the insects suffered no discernible population loss, which left them available as food for other survivors. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> is known as the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>. The nature of the event that caused this mass extinction has been extensively studied since the 1970s; at present, several related theories are supported by paleontologists. Though the consensus is that an impact event was the primary cause of dinosaur extinction, some scientists cite other possible causes, or support the idea that a confluence of several factors was responsible for the sudden disappearance of dinosaurs from the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ktextinction_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-ktextinction-179">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multimpacts_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-multimpacts-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinoextinction_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinoextinction-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_event">Impact event</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_crater" title="Chicxulub crater">Chicxulub crater</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="799" data-file-width="715" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg/220px-Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg/330px-Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg/440px-Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chicxulub_radar_topography.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicxulub_Crater" title="Chicxulub Crater">Chicxulub Crater</a> at the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>; the impactor that formed this crater may have caused the dinosaur extinction.</div></div></div>
<p>The asteroid collision theory, which was brought to wide attention in 1980 by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">Walter Alvarez</a> and colleagues, links the <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a> at the end of the Cretaceous period to a <a href="/wiki/Bolide" title="Bolide">bolide</a> impact approximately 66 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015-182">[181]</a></sup> Alvarez <i>et al.</i> proposed that a sudden increase in <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> levels, recorded around the world in the period's rock stratum, was direct evidence of the impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarezetal80_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarezetal80-183">[182]</a></sup> The bulk of the evidence now suggests that a <a href="/wiki/Bolide" title="Bolide">bolide</a> 5 to 15 kilometers (3.1 to 9.3 miles) wide hit in the vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> (in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>), creating the approximately 180 km (110 mi) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicxulub_Crater" title="Chicxulub Crater">Chicxulub Crater</a> and triggering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARHetal91_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARHetal91-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KPetal96_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-KPetal96-185">[184]</a></sup> Scientists are not certain whether dinosaurs were thriving or declining before the <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact event</a>. Some scientists propose that the meteorite impact caused a long and unnatural drop in Earth's atmospheric temperature, while others claim that it would have instead created an unusual heat wave. The consensus among scientists who support this theory is that the impact caused extinctions both directly (by heat from the meteorite impact) and also indirectly (via a worldwide cooling brought about when matter ejected from the impact crater reflected thermal radiation from the sun). Although the speed of extinction cannot be deduced from the fossil record alone, various models suggest that the extinction was extremely rapid, being down to hours rather than years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deccan_Traps">Deccan Traps</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a></div>
<p>Before 2000, arguments that the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> <a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">flood basalts</a> caused the <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> were usually linked to the view that the extinction was gradual, as the flood basalt events were thought to have started around 68 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">million years ago</a> and lasted for over 2 million years. However, there is evidence that two thirds of the Deccan Traps were created in only 1 million years about 66 million years ago, and so these eruptions would have caused a fairly rapid extinction, possibly over a period of thousands of years, but still longer than would be expected from a single impact event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwnjsd_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwnjsd-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duncan_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duncan-188">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> could have caused extinction through several mechanisms, including the release into the air of dust and sulfuric aerosols, which might have blocked sunlight and thereby reduced photosynthesis in plants. In addition, Deccan Trap volcanism might have resulted in carbon dioxide emissions, which would have increased the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> when the dust and aerosols cleared from the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duncan_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-Duncan-188">[187]</a></sup> Before the mass extinction of the dinosaurs, the release of <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_gas" title="Volcanic gas">volcanic gases</a> during the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> "contributed to an apparently massive global warming. Some data point to an average rise in temperature of 8 °C (14 °F) in the last half million years before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicxulub_Crater" title="Chicxulub Crater">impact [at Chicxulub]</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwnjsd_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-wwnjsd-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duncan_188-2"><a href="#cite_note-Duncan-188">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the years when the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> theory was linked to a slower extinction, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis Alvarez</a> (who died in 1988) replied that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontologists" title="Paleontologists">paleontologists</a> were being misled by <a href="/wiki/Signor%E2%80%93Lipps_effect" title="Signor–Lipps effect">sparse data</a>. While his assertion was not initially well-received, later intensive field studies of fossil beds lent weight to his claim. Eventually, most paleontologists began to accept the idea that the mass extinctions at the end of the Cretaceous were largely or at least partly due to a massive Earth impact. However, even <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">Walter Alvarez</a> has acknowledged that there were other major changes on Earth even before the impact, such as a drop in <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> and massive volcanic eruptions that produced the Indian <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a>, and these may have contributed to the extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez1997_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez1997-189">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_Paleocene_survivors">Possible Paleocene survivors</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paleocene_dinosaurs" title="Paleocene dinosaurs">Paleocene dinosaurs</a></div>
<p>Non-avian dinosaur remains are occasionally found above the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary</a>. In 2001, paleontologists Zielinski and Budahn reported the discovery of a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrosauridae" title="Hadrosauridae">hadrosaur</a> leg-bone fossil in the San Juan Basin, New Mexico, and described it as evidence of <a href="/wiki/Paleocene_dinosaurs" title="Paleocene dinosaurs">Paleocene dinosaurs</a>. The formation in which the bone was discovered has been dated to the early <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> epoch, approximately 64.5 million years ago. If the bone was not re-deposited into that <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">stratum</a> by weathering action, it would provide evidence that some dinosaur populations may have survived at least a half million years into the Cenozoic Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fassett_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fassett-190">[189]</a></sup> Other evidence includes the finding of dinosaur remains in the <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">Hell Creek Formation</a> up to 1.3 m (51 in) above the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary, representing <span style="white-space:nowrap">40<span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span> years of elapsed time. Similar reports have come from other parts of the world, including China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sloan_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan-191">[190]</a></sup> Many scientists, however, dismissed the supposed Paleocene dinosaurs as re-worked, that is, washed out of their original locations and then re-buried in much later sediments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS05_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FS05-192">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-193">[192]</a></sup> Direct dating of the bones themselves has supported the later date, with U–Pb dating methods resulting in a precise age of 64.8 ± 0.9 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fassettetall2011_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-fassettetall2011-194">[193]</a></sup> If correct, the presence of a handful of dinosaurs in the early Paleocene would not change the underlying facts of the extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS05_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-FS05-192">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_study">History of study</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_paleontology" title="History of paleontology">History of paleontology</a></div>
<p>Dinosaur fossils have been known for millennia, although their true nature was not recognized. The Chinese considered them to be <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a> <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a> and documented them as such. For example, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hua_Yang_Guo_Zhi" title="Hua Yang Guo Zhi">Hua Yang Guo Zhi</a></i>, a book written by <a href="/wiki/Chang_Qu" title="Chang Qu">Chang Qu</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Jin_Dynasty" title="Western Jin Dynasty">Western Jin Dynasty</a> (265–316), reported the discovery of dragon bones at Wucheng in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> Province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dong1992_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-dong1992-195">[194]</a></sup> Villagers in central China have long unearthed fossilized "dragon bones" for use in <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional medicines</a>, a practice that continues today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCdinobonemed_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCdinobonemed-196">[195]</a></sup> In Europe, dinosaur fossils were generally believed to be the remains of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" title="Giant (mythology)">giants</a> and other biblical creatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2000_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2000-197">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholarly descriptions of what would now be recognized as dinosaur bones first appeared in the late 17th century in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. Part of a bone, now known to have been the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> of a <i><a href="/wiki/Megalosaurus" title="Megalosaurus">Megalosaurus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAS97_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-WAS97-198">[197]</a></sup> was recovered from a limestone quarry at Cornwell near <a href="/wiki/Chipping_Norton" title="Chipping Norton">Chipping Norton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">Oxfordshire</a>, in 1676. The fragment was sent to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Plot" title="Robert Plot">Robert Plot</a>, Professor of Chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> and first curator of the <a href="/wiki/Ashmolean_Museum" title="Ashmolean Museum">Ashmolean Museum</a>, who published a description in his <i>Natural History of Oxfordshire</i> in 1677. He correctly identified the bone as the lower extremity of the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> of a large animal, and recognized that it was too large to belong to any known species. He therefore concluded it to be the thigh bone of a giant human similar to those mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>. In 1699, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">Edward Lhuyd</a>, a friend of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Isaac_Newton" title="Sir Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a>, was responsible for the first published scientific treatment of what would now be recognized as a dinosaur when he described and named a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropod" title="Sauropod">sauropod</a> <a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">tooth</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Rutellum" title="Rutellum">Rutellum implicatum</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L99_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-L99-199">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DS02_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-DS02-200">[199]</a></sup> that had been found in Caswell, near Witney, Oxfordshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G45_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-G45-201">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Buckland_detail.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="130" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/William_Buckland_detail.png" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Buckland_detail.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Buckland" title="William Buckland">William Buckland</a></div></div></div>
<p>Between 1815 and 1824, the Rev <a href="/wiki/William_Buckland" title="William Buckland">William Buckland</a>, a professor of <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> at Oxford, collected more fossilized bones of <i>Megalosaurus</i> and became the first person to describe a dinosaur in a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_journal" title="Scientific journal">scientific journal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAS97_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-WAS97-198">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buckland1824_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-buckland1824-202">[201]</a></sup> The second dinosaur genus to be identified, <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i>, was discovered in 1822 by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ann_Mantell" title="Mary Ann Mantell">Mary Ann Mantell</a> – the wife of English geologist <a href="/wiki/Gideon_Mantell" title="Gideon Mantell">Gideon Mantell</a>. Gideon Mantell recognized similarities between his fossils and the bones of modern <a href="/wiki/Iguana" title="Iguana">iguanas</a>. He published his findings in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GM25_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-GM25-203">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDS97_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-HDS97-204">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>The study of these "great fossil lizards" soon became of great interest to European and American scientists, and in 1842 the English paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a> coined the term "dinosaur". He recognized that the remains that had been found so far, <i>Iguanodon</i>, <i>Megalosaurus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hylaeosaurus" title="Hylaeosaurus">Hylaeosaurus</a></i>, shared a number of distinctive features, and so decided to present them as a distinct taxonomic group. With the backing of <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Albert</a>, the husband of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, Owen established the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum, London</a>, to display the national collection of dinosaur fossils and other biological and geological exhibits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owen94_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-owen94-205">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1858, <a href="/wiki/William_Parker_Foulke" title="William Parker Foulke">William Parker Foulke</a> discovered the first known American dinosaur, in <a href="/wiki/Marl" title="Marl">marl</a> pits in the small town of <a href="/wiki/Haddonfield,_New_Jersey" title="Haddonfield, New Jersey">Haddonfield, New Jersey</a>. (Although fossils had been found before, their nature had not been correctly discerned.) The creature was named <i><a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurus" title="Hadrosaurus">Hadrosaurus foulkii</a></i>. It was an extremely important find: <i>Hadrosaurus</i> was one of the first nearly complete dinosaur skeletons found (<a href="/wiki/Iguanodon#Gideon_Mantell,_Sir_Richard_Owen,_and_the_discovery_of_dinosaurs" title="Iguanodon">the first</a> was in 1834, in Maidstone, England), and it was clearly a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">bipedal</a> creature. This was a revolutionary discovery as, until that point, most scientists had believed dinosaurs walked on four feet, like other lizards. Foulke's discoveries sparked a wave of dinosaur mania in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weishampel06_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-weishampel06-206">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:268px;max-width:268px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edcope.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="465" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Edcope.jpg/130px-Edcope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Edcope.jpg/195px-Edcope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Edcope.jpg/260px-Edcope.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Edward_Drinker_Cope" title="Edward Drinker Cope">Edward Drinker Cope</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="343" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg/130px-OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg/195px-OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg/260px-OthnielCharlesMarsh.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh" title="Othniel Charles Marsh">Othniel Charles Marsh</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
