<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1408" data-file-width="2269" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png/220px-Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png/330px-Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png/440px-Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stego-marsh-1896-US_geological_survey.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marsh's 1896 illustration of the bones of <i><a href="/wiki/Stegosaurus" title="Stegosaurus">Stegosaurus</a></i>, a dinosaur he described and named in 1877</div></div></div>
<p>Dinosaur mania was exemplified by the fierce rivalry between <a href="/wiki/Edward_Drinker_Cope" title="Edward Drinker Cope">Edward Drinker Cope</a> and <a href="/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh" title="Othniel Charles Marsh">Othniel Charles Marsh</a>, both of whom raced to be the first to find new dinosaurs in what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Bone_Wars" title="Bone Wars">Bone Wars</a>. The feud probably originated when Marsh publicly pointed out that Cope's reconstruction of an <i><a href="/wiki/Elasmosaurus" title="Elasmosaurus">Elasmosaurus</a></i> skeleton was flawed: Cope had inadvertently placed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plesiosaur" title="Plesiosaur">plesiosaur</a>'s head at what should have been the animal's tail end. The fight between the two scientists lasted for over 30 years, ending in 1897 when Cope died after spending his entire fortune on the dinosaur hunt. Marsh 'won' the contest primarily because he was better funded through a relationship with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Geological_Survey" title="US Geological Survey">US Geological Survey</a>. Unfortunately, many valuable dinosaur specimens were damaged or destroyed due to the pair's rough methods: for example, their diggers often used <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">dynamite</a> to unearth bones (a method modern paleontologists would find appalling). Despite their unrefined methods, the contributions of Cope and Marsh to paleontology were vast: Marsh unearthed 86 new species of dinosaur and Cope discovered 56, a total of 142 new species. Cope's collection is now at the <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>, while Marsh's is on display at the <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">Peabody Museum of Natural History</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmes_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes-207">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1897, the search for dinosaur fossils extended to every continent, including <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Polar_dinosaurs" title="South Polar dinosaurs">Antarctic dinosaur</a> to be discovered, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ankylosaur" title="Ankylosaur">ankylosaurid</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Antarctopelta" title="Antarctopelta">Antarctopelta oliveroi</a></i>, was found on <a href="/wiki/James_Ross_Island" title="James Ross Island">James Ross Island</a> in 1986,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-antarctopelta_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-antarctopelta-208">[207]</a></sup> although it was 1994 before an Antarctic species, the theropod <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryolophosaurus_ellioti" title="Cryolophosaurus ellioti">Cryolophosaurus ellioti</a></i>, was formally named and described in a scientific journal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cryolophosaurus_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-cryolophosaurus-209">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Current dinosaur "hot spots" include southern South America (especially <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>) and China. China in particular has produced many exceptional <a href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaur" title="Feathered dinosaur">feathered dinosaur</a> specimens due to the unique geology of its dinosaur beds, as well as an ancient arid climate particularly conducive to fossilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzeretal1999_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzeretal1999-161">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".22Dinosaur_renaissance.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Dinosaur_renaissance"'>"Dinosaur renaissance"</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_renaissance" title="Dinosaur renaissance">Dinosaur renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="907" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg/220px-Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg/330px-Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg/440px-Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Bob_Bakker_with_Dino.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">Robert T. Bakker</a> with mounted skeleton of a tyrannosaurid (<i><a href="/wiki/Gorgosaurus" title="Gorgosaurus">Gorgosaurus libratus</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>The field of dinosaur research has enjoyed a surge in activity that began in the 1970s and is ongoing. This was triggered, in part, by <a href="/wiki/John_Ostrom" title="John Ostrom">John Ostrom</a>'s discovery of <i><a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">Deinonychus</a></i>, an active predator that may have been <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">warm-blooded</a>, in marked contrast to the then-prevailing image of dinosaurs as sluggish and <a href="/wiki/Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm">cold-blooded</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate_paleontology" title="Vertebrate paleontology">Vertebrate paleontology</a> has become a global <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>. Major new dinosaur discoveries have been made by paleontologists working in previously unexploited regions, including <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, South America, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, and most significantly China (the amazingly well-preserved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaurs" title="Feathered dinosaurs">feathered dinosaurs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161208_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161208-6">[5]</a></sup> in China have further consolidated the link between dinosaurs and their living descendants, modern birds). The widespread application of <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistics</a>, which rigorously analyzes the relationships between biological organisms, has also proved tremendously useful in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_classification" title="Scientific classification">classifying</a> dinosaurs. Cladistic analysis, among other modern techniques, helps to compensate for an often incomplete and fragmentary fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bakker86_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-bakker86-210">[209]</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soft_tissue_and_DNA">Soft tissue and DNA</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg/220px-Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg/330px-Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg/440px-Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scipionyx_samniticus_232.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Scipionyx" title="Scipionyx">Scipionyx</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Civico_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Milano" title="Museo Civico di Storia Naturale di Milano">Natural History Museum of Milan</a>, Italy</div></div></div>
<p>One of the best examples of soft-tissue impressions in a fossil dinosaur was discovered in <a href="/wiki/Pietraroja" title="Pietraroja">Pietraroia, Italy</a>. The discovery was reported in 1998, and described the specimen of a small, very young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coelurosaur" title="Coelurosaur">coelurosaur</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scipionyx_samniticus" title="Scipionyx samniticus">Scipionyx samniticus</a></i>. The fossil includes portions of the intestines, colon, liver, muscles, and windpipe of this immature dinosaur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-softtissue_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-softtissue-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the March 2005 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, the paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Mary_Higby_Schweitzer" title="Mary Higby Schweitzer">Mary Higby Schweitzer</a> and her team announced the discovery of flexible material resembling actual soft tissue inside a 68-million-year-old <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus rex</a></i> leg bone from the <a href="/wiki/Hell_Creek_Formation" title="Hell Creek Formation">Hell Creek Formation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>. After recovery, the tissue was rehydrated by the science team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzer2005_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer2005-70">[69]</a></sup> When the fossilized bone was treated over several weeks to remove mineral content from the fossilized bone-marrow cavity (a process called demineralization), Schweitzer found evidence of intact structures such as <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_matrix" title="Bone matrix">bone matrix</a>, and connective tissue (bone fibers). Scrutiny under the microscope further revealed that the putative dinosaur soft tissue had retained fine structures (microstructures) even at the cellular level. The exact nature and composition of this material, and the implications of Schweitzer's discovery, are not yet clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzer2005_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzer2005-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, a team including Schweitzer announced that, using even more careful methodology, they had duplicated their results by finding similar soft tissue in a <a href="/wiki/Hadrosaurid" title="Hadrosaurid">duck-billed dinosaur</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brachylophosaurus_canadensis" title="Brachylophosaurus canadensis">Brachylophosaurus canadensis</a></i>, found in the <a href="/wiki/Judith_River_Formation" title="Judith River Formation">Judith River Formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>. This included even more detailed tissue, down to preserved bone cells that seem even to have visible remnants of nuclei and what seem to be red blood cells. Among other materials found in the bone was <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>, as in the <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> bone. The type of collagen an animal has in its bones varies according to its DNA and, in both cases, this collagen was of the same type found in modern chickens and ostriches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extraction of ancient DNA from dinosaur fossils has been reported on two separate occasions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bone_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-bone-212">[211]</a></sup> upon further inspection and <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a>, however, neither of these reports could be confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wangetal1997_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wangetal1997-213">[212]</a></sup> However, a functional <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptide</a> involved in the vision of a theoretical dinosaur has been inferred using analytical phylogenetic reconstruction methods on gene sequences of related modern species such as reptiles and birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-changetal2002_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-changetal2002-214">[213]</a></sup> In addition, several <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweitzeretal1997_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweitzeretal1997-215">[214]</a></sup> have putatively been detected in dinosaur fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emberyatal2003_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emberyatal2003-216">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, researchers reported finding structures similar to blood cells and collagen fibers, preserved in the bone fossils of six Cretaceous dinosaur specimens, which are approximately 75 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="704" data-file-width="1024" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg/220px-Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg/330px-Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg/440px-Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iguanodon_Crystal_Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Outdated <i><a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">Iguanodon</a></i> statues created by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Waterhouse_Hawkins" title="Benjamin Waterhouse Hawkins">Benjamin Waterhouse Hawkins</a> for the Crystal Palace Park in 1853</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg/220px-Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg/330px-Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg/440px-Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triceratops-vs-T-Rex001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The battles that may have occurred between <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus rex</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Triceratops" title="Triceratops">Triceratops</a></i> are a recurring theme in <a href="/wiki/Popular_science" title="Popular science">popular science</a> and dinosaurs' depiction in culture.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions">Cultural depictions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_dinosaurs" title="Cultural depictions of dinosaurs">Cultural depictions of dinosaurs</a></div>
<p>By human standards, dinosaurs were creatures of fantastic appearance and often enormous size. As such, they have captured the popular imagination and become an enduring part of human culture. Entry of the word "dinosaur" into the common <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> reflects the animals' cultural importance: in English, "dinosaur" is commonly used to describe anything that is impractically large, obsolete, or bound for extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m-w_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-m-w-220">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public enthusiasm for dinosaurs first developed in <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> England, where in 1854, three decades after the first scientific descriptions of dinosaur remains, a menagerie of lifelike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_dinosaurs" title="Crystal Palace dinosaurs">dinosaur sculptures</a> were unveiled in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_Park" title="Crystal Palace Park">Crystal Palace Park</a>. The Crystal Palace dinosaurs proved so popular that a strong market in smaller replicas soon developed. In subsequent decades, dinosaur exhibits opened at parks and <a href="/wiki/Museum#Natural_history_museums" title="Museum">museums</a> around the world, ensuring that successive generations would be introduced to the animals in an immersive and exciting way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-torrens1993_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-torrens1993-221">[220]</a></sup> Dinosaurs' enduring popularity, in its turn, has resulted in significant public funding for dinosaur science, and has frequently spurred new discoveries. In the United States, for example, the competition between museums for public attention led directly to the <a href="/wiki/Bone_Wars" title="Bone Wars">Bone Wars</a> of the 1880s and 1890s, during which a pair of feuding paleontologists made enormous scientific contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-breithaupt1997_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-breithaupt1997-222">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>The popular preoccupation with dinosaurs has ensured their appearance in <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">media</a>. Beginning in 1852 with a passing mention in <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Bleak_House" title="Bleak House">Bleak House</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bleakhouse_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-bleakhouse-223">[222]</a></sup> dinosaurs have been featured in large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fictional</a> works. <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>'s 1864 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_Center_of_the_Earth" title="Journey to the Center of the Earth">Journey to the Center of the Earth</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Sir Arthur Conan Doyle">Sir Arthur Conan Doyle</a>'s 1912 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(Conan_Doyle_novel)" title="The Lost World (Conan Doyle novel)">The Lost World</a></i>, the iconic 1933 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_picture" title="Motion picture">film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933_film)" title="King Kong (1933 film)">King Kong</a></i>, the 1954 <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i> and its many sequels, the best-selling 1990 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(novel)" title="Jurassic Park (novel)">Jurassic Park</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Crichton" title="Michael Crichton">Michael Crichton</a> and its 1993 <a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)" title="Jurassic Park (film)">film adaptation</a> are just a few notable examples of dinosaur appearances in fiction. Authors of general-interest <a href="/wiki/Non-fiction" title="Non-fiction">non-fiction</a> works about dinosaurs, including some prominent paleontologists, have often sought to use the animals as a way to educate readers about science in general. Dinosaurs are ubiquitous in <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a>; numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Company_(law)" title="Company (law)">companies</a> have referenced dinosaurs in printed or televised advertisements, either in order to sell their own products or in order to characterize their rivals as slow-moving, dim-witted, or obsolete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFGlut1997_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-DFGlut1997-224">[223]</a></sup>
</p>

London: 60–204.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Report+of+the+Eleventh+Meeting+of+the+British+Association+for+the+Advancement+of+Science%3B+Held+at+Plymouth+in+July+1841&amp;rft.atitle=Report+on+British+fossil+reptiles.+Part+II.&amp;rft.pages=60-204&amp;rft.date=1841&amp;rft.aulast=Owen&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3Ddy5LAAAAYAAJ%26pg%3DPA60&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADinosaur"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/> <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=dy5LAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA103" rel="nofollow">From p. 103:</a>  "The combination of such characters … will, it is presumed, be deemed sufficient ground for establishing a distinct tribe or sub-order of Saurian Reptiles, for which I would propose the name of Dinosauria*. </div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p><p><b>General</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://www.dinodatabase.com/" rel="nofollow">DinoDatabase.com | Hundreds of dinosaurs and dinosaur related topics</a></li></ul>
<p><b>Images</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://www.gspauldino.com/" rel="nofollow">The Science and Art of Gregory S. Paul</a> Influential paleontologist's anatomy art and paintings</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://skeletaldrawing.com/" rel="nofollow">Skeletal Drawing</a> Professional restorations of numerous dinosaurs, and discussions of dinosaur anatomy.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://lhldigital.lindahall.org/cdm/landingpage/collection/dino" rel="nofollow">"Dinosaur Discovery"</a>, a collection of images from early works on dinosaurs at the <a href="/wiki/Linda_Hall_Library" title="Linda Hall Library">Linda Hall Library</a>, in support of the exhibition, <a class="external text" href="http://dino.lindahall.org/" rel="nofollow"><i>Paper Dinosaurs, 1824–1969</i></a>.</li></ul>
<p><b>Video</b>
</p>
<ul><li>BBC Nature: <a class="external text" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/nature/life/dinosaur" rel="nofollow">Dinosaur reconstructions and expert interpretations, including <i>Walking with Dinosaurs</i></a></li>
<li>BBC Explainer – <a class="external text" href="http://vimeo.com/74636204" rel="nofollow">Dinosaurs – a complete history in 4 minutes, animation</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.scientificamerican.com/article/how-dinosaurs-got-their-start-and-met-their-end-video/" rel="nofollow">Origin, evolution and extinction of the dinosaurs</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_L._Brusatte" title="Stephen L. Brusatte">Stephen Brusatte</a> video lecture April 15, 2014</li></ul>
<p><b>Popular</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://www.nhm.ac.uk/nature-online/life/dinosaurs-other-extinct-creatures/index.html" rel="nofollow">Dinosaurs &amp; other extinct creatures</a>: From the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum</a>, a well illustrated dinosaur directory.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.dinosaurnews.org/" rel="nofollow">Dinosaurnews</a> Dinosaur-related headlines from around the world, including finds and discoveries, and many links.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.ucmp.berkeley.edu/diapsids/dinosaur.html" rel="nofollow">Dinosauria</a> From UC Berkeley Museum of Paleontology.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.livescience.com/dinosaurs/" rel="nofollow">LiveScience.com</a> Dinosaur pages</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.enchantedlearning.com/subjects/dinosaurs/" rel="nofollow">Zoom Dinosaurs</a> From Enchanted Learning. Kids' site, info pages and stats, theories, history.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.geol.umd.edu/~tholtz/dinoappendix/HoltzappendixWinter2010.pdf" rel="nofollow">Dinosaur genus list</a> contains data tables on nearly every published Mesozoic dinosaur genus as of January 2011.</li></ul>
<p><b>Technical</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://palaeo-electronica.org/" rel="nofollow"><i>Palaeontologia Electronica</i></a> From Coquina Press. Online technical journal.</li></ul>
